### --- Page 0012 ---

PREFACE.

To collect and arrange the numerous cases relative to the Law of Bonds is the object of the following pages. In pursuing this object, the early decisions have been examined and referred to, so far as they are applicable to the present law; but it will be found, upon perusing them as collected in the old abridgments, that a great portion of the questions brought before the courts have arisen from the various and unsettled terms formerly used in creating an obligation, and the ambiguity consequent thereon. But the nature of a bond being now better understood, and its form settled by practice, many of the early decisions are no longer important.

In treating of particular bonds it is not professed to enumerate every possible description; but those have been selected upon which questions most frequently arise in practice. In some instances, the cases have been collected from the reports of the courts, and in others, from the books of abridgment. The object has been to give a clear and concise statement of the law as it now stands, without unnecessary detail.

FOOTNOTES:
None

<br>

### --- Page 0013 ---

PREFACE.

Instances where the bond is so intimately associated with the subject to which it relates, that recourse must necessarily be had to the works on that subject, (as in the case of a bond given by the petitioning creditor of a bankrupt,) I have purposely omitted noticing them.

The Appendix contains Forms of common occurrence, selected from approved precedents, and by attention to which others may be readily framed, to meet particular cases. Some Declarations and Pleas, prepared in conformity with the new Rules, have been added.

1, Inner Temple Lane,
March, 1835.

ERRATA,
Page 24, line 14, after the words ‘favour of,” insert ‘ any person specially named, or one of two persons, each being.”

Page 96, line 14, for “obligee,” read “obligor.”
Page 96, line 19, for “executor,” read “executrix.”

### --- Page 0024 ---

A PRACTICAL TREATISE

ON

THE LAW OF BONDS.

CHAPTER I.

OF BONDS IN GENERAL.

A bond or obligation is an instrument under seal, whereby one person becomes bound to another, for the payment of a sum of money, or for the performance of any other act or thing. The person who is thus bound is called the obligor, and he to whom the bond is given, the obligee. And this obligation may be either by or to one or several persons.

If a bond be merely for the payment of money, without any condition in or annexed to it, it is called a simple or single bill or bond. (a) But it is in general accompanied with a condition in the nature of a deed of feoffment, the performance of which is secured by a penalty, in which case it is called a conditional bond; (b) and this condition expressly mentions the money to be paid, or other act or thing to be performed or suffered, and the particular time limited for that purpose, and if the condition be broken, the bond becomes forfeited or absolute in law.

(2) 2 Blac. Com. 340, and see (6) Co. Lit. 172, a; Shep. Touch. Morrant v. Gough, 7 B. & C. 211, 567.

<br>

### --- Page 0025 ---

Specialty debt, Requisites of a good bond. Parties to a bond. Married woman obligor.

BONDS IN GENERAL.

A bond being an acknowledgment of a debt under seal, is also called a specialty, the debt being therein particularly specified; and is of a higher nature than a contract entered into without the solemnity of a seal, and therefore by the acceptance of a bond for a simple contract debt, the debt will merge in the higher security. (c) ;

In every good bond it is essentially necessary that there be an obligor and an obligee; that there be a sum in which the former is bound; that there be words sufficient to create an obligation; and that the instrument be duly executed. (d)

In considering who may be parties to a bond or obligation it may be observed, that in general no person who is under any legal disability to contract can become an obligor, though it is otherwise of an obligee.

Thus a bond given by a married woman will neither bind her or her husband, but is absolutely void in law. (e) Nor will a divorce a mensa et thoro make any difference in this respect; but after a divorce a vinculo matrimonii, (f) or if her husband have been transported for life, or having been transported for a less period, remains abroad after his period of transportation has expired; (g) or if he be an alien and was

Kirkwall, 3 Madd. 387; and when a married woman, having a separate estate, gave a bond for payment by her executors of money lent, and after her husband's death promised that her executors should pay it; it was held that an action might be maintained against the executors on this promise of their testatrix; Lee v. And if a woman married de facto to one whom she knows to have another wife, execute a bond, it will bind her even though executed jointly with him. (£)

An infant, being under a general disability to make a deed, cannot bind himself unless the bond be given for the amount of necessaries, and in that case it must be a single bill or bond for the exact sum due; (/) for if there be a penalty with condition for payment of the sum laid out in necessaries, the bond may be avoided by plea of infancy. (£) So if the bond of an infant be conditioned for the payment of interest, the whole security will be void, (2) for an infant cannot bind himself to his prejudice. (m) Nor will a confirmation by an infant after he has attained his majority give validity to a bond originally void, unless there be an estoppel in law by an instrument of as high a nature as the obligation itself. Upon that point, however, the Court, conceiving the stipulation for the payment of interest clearly bad, gave no decisive opinion.

### --- Page 0027 ---

Lunatic or idiot obligor.

Married woman obligee.

Husband and wife obligees.

Infant, idiot, or lunatic obligees.

Corporation aggregate obligee.

BONDS IN GENERAL.

It was formerly considered that a lunatic or an idiot could not set up his incompetency to contract as a defence to action on his bond, unless the bond were given after an inquisition taken under a commission of lunacy; but the personal representatives or heir of the obligor might have taken advantage of this defence. (p) It appears, however, by the later authorities, that a defendant has been permitted to give his lunacy in evidence; (q) though it would seem in the present day such defence would not avoid a bond given for necessaries, nor in any case without the obligee, at the time of the execution of the bond, had reason to suppose the obligor of unsound mind, and had committed a fraud upon him by taking it. (r)

Although a married woman is in general incapable of executing a bond, yet a bond given to her is valid, and the interest in it will vest in her husband, who may either sue alone upon it, or his wife may be joined. But if the husband expressly disagrees to the bond, it will by his refusal lose its force and become no deed. (s)

If a bond be given to husband and wife, they will be jointly interested in it, and unless the husband in his lifetime disagrees to his wife’s right thereto, she will, after his death, be entitled to it by survivorship. (t)

So a bond may be given to an infant, an idiot, a lunatic, or an alien. (u)

A corporation aggregate may also be an obligee, (v) and a revived corporation may sue upon a bond given to the old one; for the former remains the same as the latter. (w)

BONDS IN GENERAL.

But a bond given to corporations sole, as bishops, prebendaries, &c., would enure to them in their natural capacity, as they cannot take a chattel in succession unless by custom. (z)

The obligor must be bound in a sum of money, and an omission in this respect will invalidate the bond. (a) If, however, the sum be merely improperly expressed, the Courts will in general supply the deficiency, and construe it so as to give effect to the intention of the parties, if such intention can be collected from the other parts of the instrument. (b) Thus, where the obligor acknowledged himself bound in 7,700 without any species of money being mentioned, and the condition of the bond recited that he was indebted to the obligee in various sums of money, which were all stated in pounds sterling, and that the bond was given to secure payment of those sums, it was held, that as the condition showed the species of money in which it was intended the obligee should be bound, the word pounds might be supplied in the obligatory part of the bond. (c)

No particular form of words is necessary to create a bond or obligation. Any mode of expression by which the intention of the parties clearly appears will be sufficient. Thus, if it be merely stated that I, A. T. of &c., do owe C. of &c. £10, to be paid at Easter next; this, if under seal, will amount to an obligation: or, if the words used are, I, A. of &c., "do promise, or agree,

BONDS IN GENERAL.

(d) So an instrument intended to be a statute staple, but void for that purpose on account of the omission of some requisite formality, may nevertheless be valid as an obligation. (e) And where a person admits himself indebted to another in a certain sum to be paid at a future day, and for payment thereof binds himself without mentioning to whom he is bound, the bond will notwithstanding be good, since it will be intended that he is bound to the person to whom he acknowledges the debt. (f) Modern practice, however, has adopted one particular form which is in general adhered to; so that in the present day it is seldom or ever that any question, so frequent in earlier periods, arises upon the words sufficient to create a bond.

A bond may be made either in the first or third person, for the stat. 38 Edw. 3, c. 4, only applies to obligations taken in other Courts out of the realm; and is said to have been principally intended of the Court of Rome. (g) But since the 4 Geo. 2, c. 26, all bonds must be in the English tongue and language only, and not in Latin or French, or any other tongue or language whatsoever.

The rules of the law as to the execution of deeds in general are applicable to bonds. They must be written or printed on paper, vellum, or parchment, and it is essentially necessary that they be sealed, but it is important to note that the seal is not indispensable. A corporation may seal with any other seal besides their common seal. (4) But though it is usual and advisable to have a several and distinct seal for each person, one piece of wax will suffice for several obligors. (é) And where one of two partners executed a deed in the presence of the other with his consent, and as their joint act, but there was only one seal affixed, and it did not even appear that the seal had been twice put upon the wax, it was allowed to be the deed of both partners. (£) Neither is it requisite that the obligor should himself seal the bond, but any thing that amounts to a recognition by him of an impression already made to be his seal is sufficient. (2) And though several persons are intended to be jointly bound, yet if only one of them seal the bond, it will be good as against him, and may be pleaded as the several bond of that person. And where an individual executed an instrument as the joint and several bond of himself and another party, without having any authority from such party so to do, it was held to be good, as the several bond of the individual who had executed it. (m)

There are instances of bonds executed abroad, as Jamaica bonds, which do not, according to the custom of the country, require a seal, but some other formality is substituted. In these cases the obligee may, upon producing evidence of such custom, recover on the bonds in this country. (n)

Signing. Delivery. Escrow.

BONDS IN GENERAL.

Though it is usual for the obligor to sign the bond, his signature was not at common law necessary to its validity; (0) and it is apprehended the 29 Car. 2, c. 3, has made no alteration in this respect, since that statute applies only to agreements, and not to deeds. (p)

No particular form is necessary for the delivery of a bond, the mere throwing it on a table, or any act or word from which the intention of the obligor to put the bond in the possession of the obligee may be inferred, is sufficient. (g) And if the obligor deliver a bond in the presence of a witness, as his deed, but retain it in his possession, and there is nothing to show ( ) that he did not intend it to operate immediately, it will be valid and effectual, notwithstanding it was not handed over to the obligee. (r) A delivery to a third person for the use of the obligee makes the bond effective from the instant of such delivery, although the person to whom the bond is delivered be not the agent of the obligee. (s) And in the case of a corporation an actual delivery is not requisite; but the affixing the corporate seal will amount to a delivery. (¢)

A bond delivered and given to a third party may * operate as an escrow, if it be agreed, at the time of the execution, that it shall not operate until a given event happen; although such intention was not expressed at the time of delivery. (u) But if the delivery be to the obligee himself as a bond upon conditions performed, (7) Doe d. Garnons v. Knight, 5 & C. 8%; 3 D. & R. 278, 58. C. B. & C., 671.

BONDS IN GENERAL.

In the older cases, it is laid down that the bond of Redelivery, a married woman, being altogether void, may be rendered valid by a second delivery after she is sole; but if an infant, or a man by duress of imprisonment, execute a bond, and the former after he is of age, or the latter released from prison, redeliver it, the second delivery will be of no effect, as in these instances the bonds are not void but merely voidable. (y)

A bond may be good though it be not dated, or bear a false or impossible date; for all deeds take effect from, and have relation to, the time of delivery, and not the date: (z) thus, a bond bearing date in the minority of an infant, if sealed and delivered by him when he attained his majority, will bind him. (a) And where the time of the execution of the bond is material to the plaintiff’s right of action, as in the case of a release pleaded after the date, but which was executed before the delivery of the bond; it may be shown that the bond was executed on a different day from that on which it bears date.

Bond with Condition.

Condition, where written.

Effect of the omission of concluding words,

CHAPTER II.

OF BONDS WITH CONDITION.

Section 1. Of the Nature and Requisites of a good Condition, and therein of Conditions unlawful at Common Law or by Statute.

We have hitherto considered the nature and requisites of a single bill or bond; but, as before stated, a bond generally contains a condition specifying the particular sum of money to be paid, or other act to be performed ; and this condition may be contained either in the same or another instrument, or it may be indorsed on the back of the obligation; (a) but it is usually written underneath, as follows:—The condition of this obligation is such, that if the above bounden A. do and shall (stating the terms of the condition), then the above obligation to be void and of none effect, otherwise to be and remain in full force and virtue. (d)

The omission of the concluding words, “then this obligation to be void,” &c., will not render the condition bad, for they are immaterial; (c) and where by mistake

(a) Shep. Touch. 371; Vin. Ab. dition, (F) G.

Fait (G). (c) Maulevercr v. Hauaby, 2

(b) See further as to what words make a condition, Vin. Ab, Con- 371.

<br>

### --- Page 0034 ---

REQUISITES OF A GOOD CONDITION.

The concluding words were, “then the condition to be void,” it was held they might be rejected as surplusage. (d)

To constitute a good and valid condition, it is necessary that it be for the performance of an act that is lawful, and capable of being performed; it must be sensible and certain, (e) and in no way repugnant to the obligation.

If the bond be conditioned to perform an act that is impossible, as to pay money at a day past, (f) or to render a person in execution who has once been discharged, (g) the condition will be void, and the bond remain single; provided the condition is underwritten, or indorsed; (h) but if it be incorporated with the obligatory part of the bond, the instrument will be altogether void. (i) If, however, the act be merely improbable, the condition is good, however unlikely it may be. (j)

If the condition be for the performance of two distinct acts, one of which is impossible, and therefore void, the obligor will nevertheless be bound to perform the other. (k) But where both acts are possible at the time of the execution of the bond, and the obligor may do either, and one becomes impossible by the act of the obligee, or the act of God, the performance will be altogether excused. (l)

(d) Mauleverer v. Haurby, 2 Saund. 79; Bac. Ab. Condition, 173 ; Duvergier v. Fellowes, 2 M. & P. 411.

(e) Com. Dig. Condition, D 1; Bac. Ab. Condition, L.

(f) Barrett v. Fletcher, Brownlow, 104.

(g) Da Costa v. Davis, 1 B. & P. 242; and see Shep. Touch. 372 ; Bac. Ab. Condition, M.

(h) Pullerton v. Agnew, 1 Salk.

(i) Com. Dig. Condition, D 2.

(k) Campbell v. French, 6 T. R. 211.

(l) Da Costa v. Davis, 1 B. & P. 242.

(m) Com. Dig. Oblig. K 2; Duvergier v. Fellowes, 2 M. & P. 411.

### --- Page 0035 ---

Uncertain or insensible condition,

Repugnant condition.

Distinction between the effect of impossible and unlawful condition,

REQUISITES OF A GOOD CONDITION.
Where the condition of a bond is insensible, as if it commences “Whereas the above bounden, &c. shall and will,” instead of “if,” (m) or is so uncertain that its meaning cannot be ascertained, the condition will be void, and the obligation binding. (n) But though the condition be defective in terms, the Courts will endeavour to give effect to the intent of the parties, if it can be ascertained from the whole instrument, and they will accordingly transpose, disjoin, and supply words. (o)
Where the condition was, that if the obligor should pay £10 in manner and form following, that is to say, £5 upon four several days therein named, but if default shall be made in any of the payments the said obligation shall be void, or otherwise to stand in full force and virtue; and the defendant pleaded that on such a day he did not pay £5, the Court upon demurrer gave judgment for the plaintiff. (p)

When the condition is repugnant to the obligation, as if it be that the obligee shall not have the benefit of, or at any time sue for, the money in the obligation, the condition will be void and the obligation binding. (q)

There is a distinction to be observed between the effect of a condition that is impossible, uncertain, insensible, or repugnant, and one that is unlawful. In the former cases the condition alone is void, and the bond becomes single, but in the latter instance not only the condition, but the obligation also is void.

The condition of a bond may be unlawful, either

UNLAWFUL CONDITIONS.

(s)

And it may be observed, that in all cases where the illegality does not appear upon the face of the bond, the true nature of the transaction may be disclosed by pleading, notwithstanding the statement in the plea is at variance with the terms of the condition, and it is not necessary, in such case, to aver that the condition is colourable to conceal the illegal transaction, or to negative the terms of the condition. (t)

As no contract founded upon an immoral consideration is binding, a bond conditioned to pay money in consideration of the obligor and obligee cohabiting together, may be avoided. (u) But though a bond, the consideration of which is future cohabitation, is not binding, yet it will be valid if given at the determination of the illicit intercourse; for in that case the sum thereby secured will be considered as a compensation for the injury done to the woman. (a) And the circumstance of the obligor having been married at the time of his cohabitation with the obligee, will make no difference.

14 UNLAWFUL CONDITIONS.

In this respect, for a court of law will not distinguish between the different degrees of immorality; but whether or no at the time the bond was given there was any intention or agreement to continue the connection for the future, is a question for the consideration of a jury.

In restraint of trade. All bonds in general restraint of trade are unlawful. This subject was fully examined in the case of Mitchell v. Reynolds, and the rules of law there laid down have been since recognized and adopted by several succeeding decisions. From that case it appears that a general restraint not to exercise a trade throughout the kingdom is void, being for the benefit of neither party, and only oppressive. But if there be a good and adequate consideration for the restraint, and it be confined to a particular district, it will be valid. Where a surgeon agreed to take an assistant in his business for so long time as the former thought fit, and the assistant agreed not to practise on his own account for fourteen years within ten miles of the place where his employer lived; it was held there was a sufficient consideration for the restraint.

How far the restraint may be extended. It does not appear from the authorities that there is any settled rule as to what distance the restraint may be extended. In the last mentioned case the limits within which the obligor engaged not to practise were considered reasonable; and where the defendant gave a bond, conditioned not to set up as a surgeon and man-midwife in the town of Aylesbury, or within 133, 9D. & R. 165, 8 C. Bonds in restraint of marriage are illegal: thus, where a bond was given by a widow, conditioned to pay the obligee, if she should marry again, £100, eight days after her marriage; and the obligee at the same time gave her a counter bond, conditioned to pay her executor £100, provided she remained unmarried until her death; and she having married, a bill was brought in Chancery to have the bond delivered up; it was decreed to be delivered up and cancelled, as being in restraint of marriage. (/)

So bonds entered into for procuring marriage, or given after marriage, according to a promise made before, are illegal. (g)

And where a bond is given for the performance of any illegal act, as to commit a trespass, unlawfully maintain a suit, or drop a criminal prosecution; or if given to secure the return of a person unlawfully impressed, upon payment of the money for which his services were commuted; or secure the price of goods sold where the traffic was illegal; or if the condition

Bond in lieu of other security, Instances of bonds not illegal.

be to perform covenants which are void at common law, or by statute; (m) or to pay money upon the obligee's procuring subscriptions for shares in a company to be formed of many persons, for the purposes of becoming assignees of a patent containing a proviso to be void if assigned to more than five persons; (n) in all these and the like cases the bond may be avoided.

In like manner, a bond given in lieu of another security, founded upon an illegal consideration, of which the obligee had notice, will be void. (o) But if the obligee were not aware of the illegality of the original consideration, the bond will not be affected by it. (p)

But a bond good at common law, and given to the treasurer of a friendly society, will not be void because the rules of that society are not registered according to the provisions of a particular statute. (q) And a bond executed by the servant of a company, conditioned to take possession of the effects of persons dying intestate in the settlements of the company, and to sell the same, and to remit the produce to the company in Europe, to be by them delivered to the lawful administrator, is a legal bond. (r) Also, where a bond was given by an individual, conditioned to be void if the obligor (on the obligee’s agreeing not to prosecute him) should remove certain public nuisances, and not erect others, the consideration was held to be good in law. These statutes, prohibiting the sale of offices, being rather remedial than penal statutes, are not confined to offices which were in existence at the time of their enactment, but will apply equally to those which have been since created. (m)

Where an office is within the meaning of these statutes, and the salary is certain, if the principal appoint a deputy, and take from him a bond conditioned for the payment of a less sum; or if where the profits are uncertain, arising from fees, if the principal reserve a smaller sum out of the fees, the bond is not illegal. But where the reservation or agreement is not to pay out of the profits, but to pay a general or certain sum at all events, a bond conditioned for the payment of such sum is void. (n)

And if an office be within the provisions of the above-mentioned statutes, a bond conditioned for the assignment of the profits of the office would be equally void. (o) Also, if a bond be given with condition that the obligor shall pay the obligee a share of the profits of an office which the former shall be appointed to through the interest of the obligee, and this be without the knowledge of the person who has the right of appointing to the office, it is such a fraud on him as will avoid the bond. (p)

All bonds given upon any simoniacal contract are illegal and void. The statutes relating to this subject

By the first-mentioned statute it is enacted, “that if any person or persons, or bodies corporate, shall for money, reward, gift, profit, or benefit, directly or indirectly, or for or by reason of any promise, agreement, grant, bond, covenant, or other assurance, of or for any sum of money, directly or indirectly, present or collate any person to any benefice with cure of souls, dignity, prebend, or living ecclesiastical, or bestow the same for any such corrupt consideration, every such presentation, (andc.) and every admission, institution, investiture, and induction thereupon, shall be utterly void, frustrate, and of none effect in law.” And by the eighth section it is enacted, “that if any incumbent of any benefice with cure of souls, shall corruptly resign or exchange the same, or corruptly take for the resigning or exchanging the same, directly or indirectly, any pension, money, or benefit, that then as well the giver as the taker thereof shall lose double the value of the sum so given.”

The 12 Ann. stat. 2, c. 12, s. 2, enacts, “that if any person shall, for money, reward, gift, profit, or advantage, or for any promise, agreement, bond, (andc.) or other assurance for money, (andc.) directly or indirectly in his own name, or in the name of any other person, procure the next presentation to any ecclesiastical living, and shall be presented or collated thereupon, every such presentation and admission, &c. shall be void, and such agreement shall be deemed a simoniacal contract, and it shall be lawful for the crown to present for that turn only; and the person so corruptly accepting that living shall thenceforth be disabled to enjoy the same.

When bond given by clerk to patron good.

General resignation bond illegal.

UNLAWFUL CONDITIONS.

Merely the presentation made in consequence of or under it; yet upon the general principles of law, a bond made for the purpose of furthering an object prohibited by statute is void. (7)

It would be foreign to a treatise of this description to enter fully upon the law of simony, the present observations will therefore be confined to what is more particularly within the province of this work, viz. bonds given by clerks to their patrons upon presentation to a benefice; and it is proposed to consider in what cases such bonds are binding, and when illegal and void.

If a bond be given by a clerk to his patron on receiving a presentation to a living, conditioned for the performance of any duty which the incumbent was by law bound to perform, as if it be that the clerk shall reside upon the living, take care of the cure of souls, or keep the parsonage-house in proper repair, in all these cases the bond will be valid. (r)

It was formerly held that a bond with condition to resign at the request of patron was good upon the face of it, and could only be avoided by a plea showing it to have been originally made upon some corrupt contract not apparent on the bond itself. (s) This doctrine appeared fully established by a long series of decisions, and was considered for nearly a century and a half to be the settled law. (t) At length, however, the question was brought by writ of error before the House of Lords, and that tribunal, having fully examined the principles of the previous decisions, reversed the judgment. (q) Fletcher v. Lord Sondes, 3 227; Babbington v. Wood, Cro. Bing. 530; Bartlett v. Vinor, Carth, Car. 180; Fletcher v. Lord Sondes, 251. 3 Bingh. 506; Johnes v. Luwrence, R. 359. (t) Bishop of London v. Ffytche, 1 Stra, 492. (s) Peele v. Com. Carliol, 1 East, 248. Partridge vy. Whiston, 4 T. Cro. Jac. 359.

<br>

### --- Page 0046 ---

UNLAWFUL CONDITIONS.

Ments which the Courts of Common Pleas and King’s Bench had successively given in favour of these bonds. So that it is now settled a general resignation bond clearly falls within the statute of Elizabeth, (wu) and cannot under any circumstances be supported.

The principle established by this decision of the House of Lords was very reluctantly adopted by the courts below, (x) and a distinction was attempted to be drawn between a general and a special bond of resignation. It was contended that a bond with condition to resign in favour of a son or a near relation of the patron, was neither a violation of the policy of the law, nor within that species of benefit which the statute of Elizabeth contemplated.

This question also was settled by the House of Lords; and in the well-known case of Fletcher v. Lord Sondes, (y) it was decided that a bond with condition to resign in favour of one of two brothers of the patron, was within the principle which governed the case of The Bishop of London v. Efytche, and therefore illegal.

The effect of this decision being to avoid all special resignation-bonds, which before then had been executed upon the supposition that such securities were legal, and to render the parties to them liable to the penalties of a simoniacal contract; the legislature, for the purpose of affording protection to those who had

This statute having thus provided an indemnity for the serious consequences likely to result from the decision of the House of Lords, a subsequent statute (a) was passed rendering valid all engagements to resign any benefice in favour of an uncle, son, grandson, brother, nephew, or grandnephew of the patron or of one of the patrons by blood or marriage, not being merely a trustee; provided the engagement is entered into before the presentation, nomination, collation, or appointment of the party entering into the same, and provided also that one part of the instrument or writing by which the engagement shall be made is deposited within the space of two months in the office of the registrar of the diocese wherein the benefice is situate.

The above statute of Elizabeth also renders it illegal to receive money for electing any officer, fellow, scholar, or other person, to any place in collegiate or cathedral churches, colleges, schools, halls, hospitals, or societies: and prohibits the giving and taking money for resigning a place in such churches, colleges, (&c.,)(b) and consequently all bonds founded on such transactions are void.

This statute having made stock-jobbing contracts and agreements illegal, all bonds in respect of such transactions are void. But the statute has been construed to apply only to the stock of this country, and therefore a dealing in Columbian bonds is not within its meaning.

UNLAWFUL CONDITIONS.

It has now been clearly decided, in opposition to former opinions, (c) that money lent, and applied by the borrower for the express purpose of settling losses on a stockjobbing transaction, cannot be recovered back, although the lender was not a party to the illegal contract. (d)

To debt on bond, the defendant pleaded that he, by his broker, made unlawful contracts for the purchase of shares in the public funds, and the broker having voluntarily paid £500 to compound the difference, the defendant, for the purpose of securing the repayment of that sum, gave the broker his promissory note, which was indorsed after it became due to the plaintiffs; that the plaintiffs threatened to sue the defendant on the note; and that the defendant, in fear of that suit, gave the bond, which the plaintiffs accepted in lieu of the note, well knowing that the note had been given as an illegal consideration. It was held that both the note and bond were void. (e)

By 9 Ann. c. 14, s. 1, “all bonds executed by any person, where the whole or any part of the consideration is for money, or other valuable things won by gaming, or playing at cards, dice, tennis, bowls, or other game, or by betting on the sides or hands of such as game at any of the said games, or for repaying any money knowingly lent or advanced for such gaming or betting, or lent or advanced at the time and place of such play to any person so gaming or betting, or that shall, during such play, so game or bet, shall be utterly void, frustrate, and of none effect, to all intents and purposes.” (c) Petrie v. UNLAWFUL CONDITIONS.

Under the above statute, it is immaterial whether the game is lawful or not, for all bonds given for money lost at games either of skill or chance are void.

All bonds given in consideration of a usurious contract are void.

The previous statutes relating to this subject were repealed by 37 Hen. 8, c. 9, which limited the legal rate of interest to 10 per cent. per annum. This statute was repealed by 5 & 6 Edw. 6, c. 20, but was revived by the 13 Eliz. c. 8, which repealed the statute of Edward. By the 21 Jac. c. 17, s. 2, the legal rate of interest was reduced to 8 per cent. per annum, and this rate of interest was subsequently reduced to 6 per cent. The 12 Ann. st.2,c. 16, s. 2, which at present regulates the rate of interest, enacts—1. That no person upon any contract shall take, accept, or receive for the loan of money or other commodities, above the rate of 5 per cent. per annum, under penalty of forfeiture of treble the money lent. 2. That all bonds, contracts, and assurances for payment of any principal or money to be lent or covenanted to be performed upon or for any usury, whereupon or whereby there shall be reserved or taken above the sum of 5/. in the hundred, shall be utterly void.

To constitute usury there must be a loan or forbearance, and as to illegal games, see Hodson v. Botham, 1 C. & M. 797; Squires v. Bann, 4 T.R. 1; Whaley v. Pajet, 3 Camp. 140; Brown v. 2 B. & P. 51; Ximenes v. Jaques, Cowp. 281; Blackton v. 6 T. R. 499.

Usury.

Footnotes:
(f) Sigel v. Jebb, 3 Starke. 1; Pye, 2 Wils. 430; Lynol v. Long

### --- Page 0051 ---

UNLAWFUL CONDITIONS.

An acceptance of a bill of exchange discounting his own acceptances at a premium beyond the legal rate of interest; and therefore, if the acceptor of a bill of exchange discount his own acceptances at a premium beyond the legal rate of interest, it is usury. And a contract with a corrupt intent to take illegal interest is usury.

If principal and interest be endangered it is not usury.

Loan of money's worth, a premium beyond the legal rate of interest, it is not usury. And if an excess in charge be bond fide made with reference to the trouble, expense, and inconvenience incurred by the lender of money in the nature of commission, and not with regard to the interest, the contract is not usurious.

There must further be a contract with a corrupt intent to take illegal interest; thus, if interest beyond the legal amount be reserved by mistake, or by an error in the computation of time, it is not usury.

If the money be advanced upon a contingency, and both principal and interest are subject to risk, it is not usury. Thus, post obit, bottomry, or respondentia bonds are not illegal however extortionate the terms of them may be. And the reservation in a bond of a greater sum than is allowed for interest for the non-payment of the principal at the end of the year, is not usurious, because it is in the power of the obligor to avoid the payment of the money reserved, by paying the principal at the day appointed.

The statute is not confined to a loan of money, but also extends to money's worth. Thus, usury may be committed by advancing goods to be paid for in money, and if the terms are such that they exceed the legal rate of interest, it is usury.

(q)

To avoid a bond on the ground of usury, the contract must have been usurious at the time the bond was given; for if the bond were good at its creation, the subsequent acceptance of illegal interest will not avoid it; though the obligee will thereby render himself liable to the penalty given by the statute. (r) Thus, if a bond be given for the repayment of money with interest at 5 per cent., proof that the obligee has received interest on it at 73 per cent. will not avoid the bond, unless the jury are satisfied that it was agreed, at or before the execution of the bond, that more than 5 per cent. should be paid. (s)

A bond given in lieu of another security, tainted with usury, will be void if given either to the party to the original contract or his personal representative; but where the first security has been transferred to a person ignorant of the usurious contract, who accepts from the original debtor another security in lieu thereof, the substitute security is valid in the hands of such innocent person. (#) But where the original security is given in pursuance of an usurious contract, and the

Contract must have been usurious at the time the bond was given,

substitute security is void. (t)

Substituted security is merely for the principal and legal interest, the objectionable part having been abandoned, the latter security will not be affected by the illegality of the former one. (u)

In Ireland and the British colonies (v) the legal rate of interest frequently exceeds 5 per cent.; and in such case, if the contract be made, and the security executed, abroad, it would not be illegal here. (z) But if the bond be executed in this country, though in pursuance of a contract not illegal abroad, it will be invalid. The 3 Geo. 4, c. 47, (which repealed 1 & 2 Geo. 4, c. 51, by which the provisions of 14 Geo. 3, c. 79, were extended,) has rendered legal all bonds and other securities executed in Great Britain, for the payment of interest on mortgages of lands, tenements, hereditaments, slaves (y) cattle, or other things, in Ireland or the West India colonies, provided the interest reserved does not exceed the rate allowed by the law of the place. But this statute does not extend to personal contracts; and therefore, where A. contracted with B. for the sale of an estate in the West Indies, and it was agreed that part of the purchase money should remain secured by the bond of B. and C., which was given, reserving interest at G per cent., and that bond was afterwards cancelled, and another executed in England by B. and D., reserving the same interest as the former one, the substituted bond was held to be usurious. CONSTRUCTION OF CONDITION.

In addition to the above instances may be enumerated bonds executed for the purpose of hindering or delaying creditors or others; (a) or for inducing a creditor to sign a bankrupt’s certificate; (b) or given to the sheriff for ease and favour; (c) or on account of gaming policies on ships and lives; (d) or for the price of commodities, for the removal of which a permit is required by the excise laws; (e) or if the consideration of the bond be spirituous liquors sold in quantities of less than twenty shillings value; (f) and in all these cases the bonds are void.

Section 2.

Of the Construction of the Condition, and herein of the Recitals.

A single bill or bond is always taken most strongly against the obligor, but the condition being for his benefit is construed most strongly in his favour. (g)

In the construction of the condition the courts will look to the intention of the parties, so far as it can be collected from the instrument itself; and when the intention is manifest, they will transpose or reject insensible words and supply an accidental omission, (h) in order to give effect to it. (g) When the condition of a bond was "if the obligor did not pay a certain sum to the obligee, then the obligation to be void"; such an error was not

Other instances of bonds void by statute.

### --- Page 0055 ---

Doty of obligor and character of obligee.

CONSTRUCTION OF CONDITION.

Permitted to defeat the apparent intention. So where the father of two illegitimate children executed a bond conditioned for the payment of an annuity for the support of them and their mother during their joint lives, or in case of the death of the children during the life of the mother, and one of the children died; the Court, looking to the intent of the parties, supplied the omission of the words “survivor of them,” and held the bond to be in force so long as either of the three individuals therein named were living.

The nature of the duty of the obligor and character of the obligee will be regarded as explanatory of the intent of the parties. As where a bond was given to commissioners of sewers by a collector of rates, conditioned for rendering a faithful account and paying to the commissioners all monies already received, or which should thereafter be received, the condition was construed to extend to sums of money received by the collector by virtue of a rate made by commissioners acting under a commission which expired before the execution of the bond, such construction appearing conformable to the language of the instrument and the nature of the duty, the performance of which the bond was intended to enforce.

The condition of a bond is frequently preceded by a recital of certain explanatory facts, and in such case if a particular thing be referred to, the recital will operate against the parties to the bond as a conclusive admission of the fact recited; for it is a general rule that a party is estopped from denying that which he has admitted by his own instrument.

EFFECT OF RECITALS. 33

Has expressly admitted under his hand and seal. (0)
The recital of the receipt of certain monies due to the obligee will preclude the obligor from pleading that he never received such monies. (p) And where a particular consideration is recited the obligor cannot plead that the consideration was different, unless he can make it appear that the transaction was fraudulent and unlawful. (g) So if the bond be conditioned for the payment of a certain rent according to the terms of an indenture of lease, the obligor is estopped from pleading that the indenture was never executed, or that a less sum was reserved by the lease, though such was in fact the case. (r)

The recitals will frequently operate in restraint of condition, though the words of it import a larger liability than the recitals contemplate; (s) and more especially in favour of sureties, (t) for whom the condition is always beneficially construed. The leading authority on this subject is the case of Lord Arlington v. Merricke, (u) where the condition, after reciting that the plaintiff had deputed one T. J. to be deputy postmaster of the stage of Oxon for the term of six months, was, that if the said T. J. did and would, during all the time that he should continue deputy postmaster of the said stage, duly and faithfully execute the duties thereof, then the obligation to be void.

(p) Shelley v. Wright, Wiles, 9.
(g) Hill v. Manchester Water Works Company, 2 B. & Adol. 544.
(r) Samson v. Nemere, 3 N. & M. 608; Hosier v. Searle, 2 B. & P. 408; Doddington’s case, 2 Co. 33 b.; Thornhill v. King, Cro. Eliz. 757 ; and see 1 W. Saund. #15, n. (2).
(s) Pearsall v. Summersett, 4 Taunt. 593; and see Payler v. Homersham, 4 M. & S. 425.
(t) 2 Saund. 412.

Post, tit. Liability of Surety.

### --- Page 0057 ---

EFFECT OF RECITALS,

After oyer of the condition the defendant pleaded performance, and the plaintiff replied assigning a breach of the condition by the said T. J. after the expiration of six months from the date of the bond. To this replication the defendant demurred, on the ground that, by the intention of the condition, he was not to be responsible for T. J. for any longer time than six months, although the words of it were that during all the time that he should continue postmaster he should observe and perform the condition of the bond. The Court were of opinion that the condition referred to the recital, by which the defendant was bound only for six months, and they accordingly gave judgment for the defendant.

So where the condition, (w) after reciting that the defendant had agreed with the plaintiffs to collect and receive the several rents and other revenues of their works at Liverpool, for twelve months from the date thereof, was declared to be, that if the defendant should, from time to time and at all times during the continuance of such his employment, use due diligence in collecting and receiving all rents and sums of money which should grow and become due to the company, and should render to the company from time to time a true account in writing of all sums theretofore received on account of the said works, and should also so long as he should continue and be employed by the company, from time to time observe and perform the orders of their committee, then the said writing obligatory to be void. The plaintiffs averred that the defendant continued in their employ for a long time after the making of the writing obligatory, and during that time received divers sums of money for which he did not account ; the defendant pleaded that for twelve months from the date of the bond.

(u) Liverpool Water Works Company v. Atkinson, 6 East, 507.

<br>

of Lord Arlington v. Merricke, gave judgment for the

Again, where the bond recited that the defendant was the receiver at Bristol of a certain company,
and was conditioned for truly accounting for all sums received by him; a breach stating that he received money and did not account for it, was held bad; as it appeared by the recital that he was only bound to account
for money received at a particular place, in the exercise
of his appointment there. (v) In like manner, where the condition of a bond recited that a sheriff had constituted
the defendant bailiff of a hundred in his county, that if
the defendant should duly execute all warrants directed to him, then the obligation should be void. It
was adjudged that the words “all warrants” should
be intended only of all warrants directed to defendant
as bailiff of the said hundred. (w)

sum to a particular amount, an advance beyond that
amount will not avoid the bond, without there is an

### --- Page 0059 ---

EFFECT OF RECITALS.

The condition recited that the obligees were bankers, and that two persons therein named carried on the business of paper-manufacturers, and that the latter kept an account with the obligees, which they had overdrawn to the amount of 4822/., and that in order to enable them to carry on the business the obligees had agreed to allow them for a time to overdraw such further sums as they should require, so that the same, together with the 4822/., should not exceed in the whole at any one time 5000/., and the condition was for the payment of the sum of 4822/., and also for such further sums as the obligees might advance, not exceeding in the whole 5000/.. It was held, that the bond was not avoided by the obligees having allowed sums of money, which, together with the 4822/., exceeded 5000/., to be drawn upon them, since it did not appear from the whole instrument that there was any intention to restrict the advances to the sum of 5000/., although the liabilities of the sureties did not extend beyond that amount. (x)

When recitals refer to a statute, it should however be observed, that the doctrine established by the above-mentioned cases proceeds from an endeavour to give effect to the intention of the parties and the object for which the security was given; and therefore, where a bond is conditioned for the performance of the duties of an office, and the recitals refer to a particular statute under the provisions of which the bond is given, the recitals will not restrain the condition from extending to other duties of that office imposed by a subsequent statute in force at the time the bond was given. (y)

(2) Parker v. Wise, 6 M.& S. (y) Farr v. Hollis, 9 B. & C. 239, S15.

### --- Page 0060 ---

EFFECT OF RECITALS.

But though the recitals may operate in restraint
of the condition, they cannot affect the obligatory part
of the bond, or limit the amount of the penalty therein
contained. For in the former case the recitals are
considered as incorporate with, and forming part of
the condition; but the condition and the obligatory
part of the bond are in themselves distinct. Therefore, where a bond, executed in the penal sum of
1000/., recited that the obligor had taken a farm of
the obligee, and it had been agreed that the obligor
should enter into a bond with two sureties, in the
penal sum of 500/., for the due payment of the rent,
it was held that the liability even of the sureties was
not restricted to the amount mentioned in the recital. (z)

The condition may also be restrained by a memorandum indorsed upon the bond before its execution,
if it appear to have been the intention of the parties
that such memorandum should form part of the condition. (a) Thus, where before the execution of the bond
an indorsement was made that the obligee had given
an undertaking not to sue upon the bond until after the
obligor’s death, it was considered that this memorandum made the bond in effect payable only by the representatives of the obligor. (4)

When a day is named,
Performance before the day.

( 88 )

SecTIon 3.
Of the Performance or Breach of the Condition.

The condition of an obligation, being for the benefit of the obligor, must in general be strictly performed; (c) but it is not necessary in every case that the performance should be according to the letter of the condition; if the object and intent of the parties be answered, it will be sufficient.

If a particular day be named for the payment of money, or the performance of any other act; the money must be paid or other act performed on or before the day mentioned in the condition. (d)

The payment of money, or performance of any other act before the time limited for that purpose, will be a sufficient compliance with the terms of the condition, for it in effect amounts to a performance on the day; (e) and in pleading such payment or performance, it should be stated to have been made on the day mentioned in the bond, and proof of payment or performance before the day will support the plea. (f) Nor is it necessary, in every case, that there should be an actual payment of the sum due, but an arrangement of accounts may, in some instances, amount to a payment; as where a bond was given for securing an annuity, and before the first instalment became due, the obligor lent to the obligee a large sum of money, and it was thereupon agreed that the obligee should retain the payments of the annuity as they became due, out of

(g) So where the bond recited that the plaintiffs were shareholders in a certain company, and having paid 30 per cent. on their shares, had undertaken to pay up the remainder forthwith; that three individuals therein named had agreed to purchase these shares of the plaintiffs, and that the price was to be secured by the joint bond of one of them and the defendant; and the condition was for payment of the amount of these shares, together with interest thereon, from the time of the advance or payment thereof by the plaintiffs. The plaintiffs being also shareholders in another company, of which they were treasurers, and which latter company was indebted to them in a large sum of money, prevailed on the first-mentioned company to purchase goods of the latter company, and for the purpose of effecting payment thereof, without any calls having been made, entered up in their books as paid the remaining 70 per cent. due on their shares: it was held that the plaintiffs had advanced or paid the money for the shares within the terms of the condition, and were entitled to sue the defendants on the bond. (h)

40 PERFORMANCE ON BREACH OF CONDITION.

A debt shall be brought upon any single bill or upon a bond which hath a condition or defeazance, to make void the same upon payment of a lesser sum, at a day or place certain, if the obligor, his heirs, executors or administrators, have, before the action brought, paid to the obligee, his executors or administrators, the principal and interest due by the defeazance or condition of such bond, though such payment was not made strictly according to the condition or defeazance, yet it shall and may nevertheless be pleaded in bar of such action; and shall be as effectual a bar thereof as if the money had been paid at the day and place according to the condition and defeazance, and had been so pleaded.

It appears, however, that the above statute is confined to an actual payment, and that a tender and refusal of principal and interest after the day cannot be pleaded. (m)

Oyer of the bond and condition, pleaded that no demand had been made before action brought. To this plea there was a general demurrer, and it was contended on the part of the defendant, that as a party suing on a bond does not proceed for an antecedent debt, but seeks to recover a penalty occasioned by the nonperformance of the condition, it was necessary, as no day was named for payment of the money, that there should be a demand to occasion a forfeiture; since the stipulation for the payment of interest clearly showed it was the intention of the parties that the bond should not be payable immediately. But the Court were of opinion that the bond became payable upon its execution; and that the bringing the action was a sufficient demand.

But if the bond is conditioned for the payment of money, or for the performance of a collateral act on demand, an express demand seems necessary, (q) unless there was a duty to pay the money or perform the act before demand, (r) in which case the bringing the action will be considered a sufficient demand. (s)

But where the defendant borrowed money for the use of his mother, and gave a bond to pay on demand, if his mother did not, in an action of debt brought on this bond, the defendant, after craving oyer, demurred on the ground that there was no special request when and where he required the mother to pay it; the Court held there was a duty ab initio, which the law makes payable on demand, and in such case it is not necessary to allege an express demand. (2)

If the condition be for the performance of an act that is local, and to which the concurrence of both obligor and obligee is necessary, and no time is mentioned for that purpose, the obligor hath during his life to perform it, unless hastened by request. (y) But if the act, though local, be for the benefit of the obligee, and may be performed in his absence, as the acknowledgment of satisfaction on the record, it ought to be done in a reasonable time. If, however, the act neither concerns the obligee nor is for his benefit, the obligor may perform it at any time before action brought.

Where the money is to be paid, or other act to be performed to a stranger, it must be done in a reasonable time, and a tender will not save the condition if the other refuse to accept it; for where a person undertakes to do an act to a stranger, he must at his peril take care of the performance. (s)

PERFORMANCE OR BREACH OF CONDITION.

If the condition be for the performance of an act at a particular place on a day certain, it must be performed on that day; and it will be no excuse that the performance at the time and place mentioned in the condition has become impossible, if such impossibility was caused by the obligor’s neglect. Thus where the bond was conditioned for payment on the 29th of November, at Cumberland, of costs when taxed by the Master of the K. B., and it appeared that the costs were only taxed on the 25th, and notice given to the defendant on the 29th, the bond was considered forfeited, as the obligor might by taking out an order for the obligee to attend the taxation, have ascertained the amount in time to have paid them according to the term of the condition. (z)

If, however, the bond is for the payment of money, a payment before action brought, though not at the place appointed in the condition, is sufficient. (a)

Where the money is to be paid at a particular place, and no time is limited for that purpose, the obligor cannot tender the money at any time at the place appointed, but he must give the obligee notice of his intention to pay the money; (4) unless the latter has by his own act rendered such notice impossible, (c)

And where the money is to be paid at a particular place on or before a certain day; it cannot be tendered at such a place before the last day. (d)

If the bond be conditioned for the payment of money and no place is appointed for that purpose, the obligor ought to seek the obligee wherever he may be. (b)

The performance must be according to the terms of the condition, and it will be no answer to an action on the bond that the parties interested have derived more benefit from its performance in any other way. Where the obligor was bound to leave his children 200£ jointly, and he gave his eldest son an estate in land of more than the value of 50£ and his other three children 50£ a piece, to be paid when they attained the age of twenty-one, this was held not to be a performance of the condition. (/) So payment by a collector of taxes of monies received by him to the account of a different year from that for the service of which they were collected, is a breach of the condition of the bond for due payment. (g) And where a bond was conditioned for the performance of a covenant to pay the obligee certain rents, and also the increase and improvements that might or should be made thereof, upon a new contract or renewal of the lease; it was held, that a fine received upon the renewal of a lease was within the terms of the condition. (h) Also where a bond given by a father upon the marriage of his daughter was conditioned for payment of interest of a certain sum to the husband or his executors during the life of the obligor, and for payment of the principal

Within a limited time after the obligor’s death, if any of the issue of the marriage should be living at that time, and the children of the marriage had died leaving grandchildren; the grandchildren were deemed issue of the marriage within the terms of the condition. (é)

A literal compliance with the terms of the condition is not sufficient, but the performance must also be according to the intent of the parties. Thus where the bond was conditioned for the delivery by the defendant to the plaintiff, before a certain day, of an obligation in which he was bound to the defendant, and the defendant sued the plaintiff upon that obligation and recovered, and afterwards and before the appointed day delivered it to the plaintiff; the question was, if this was a performance of the condition? The Court held it was not; although the words were performed, yet the intent was not performed; for the intent was that he should have the obligation for his discharge, and notwithstanding the delivery of the bond he might have the benefit of the judgment. (%) So where the condition of the bond was that the obligor would permit his wife to make a will of his goods to the value of a certain sum, to be paid within one year after her decease, and in an action on the bond the obligor pleaded that he permitted his wife to make a will: the Court upon demurrer held the plea bad, for he should have shown that the money was paid accordingly.(/) Again, a condition to render a fair, just, and perfect account, in writing, of all sums received, is not satisfied by the mere delivery of an account in writing, but the money should also be paid to the obligee, for that is the clear

When there is a precedent act, the obligee must perform it by the time specified. The obligor's liability will not attach until its performance. (n) Where there is a precedent act to be performed on the part of the obligee, the liability of the obligor will not attach until its performance. (o) As if the bond be conditioned to do one of two things within a certain time, at the election of the obligee, he must make his election within the time limited. (p) And if the condition be that the obligor shall pay a sum of money to such person as the obligee shall name by his last will and testament, and the obligee die without naming any person, the obligor is not bound to pay it to the executors or residuary legatee of the obligee. (q) Also, where the condition was that the obligor would pay a bill of exchange within thirty days after it should be sent from Bombay to England, duly protested for want of payment, and it appeared that when the bill arrived at Bombay the drawee had left that place and his agent refused to accept it; it was held that the bond was not forfeited by the obligor refusing to pay the bill within thirty days after it was returned to England protested for non-acceptance. But when the precedent act has been performed, the liability of the obligor will attach, though he has received no notice of it. (s) And in general, when the obligor relies upon any matter in excuse of performance, he must show that he has done every thing in his power towards complying with the terms of the condition. As if the condition be to exhibit an inventory in the Ecclesiastical Court before a certain day, it is not enough for the defendant to plead that no court was held on that day, but he must also show that he was there ready. (é)

Where the obligor’s liability depends upon the performance of a previous act, which lies equally within the knowledge and privity of the obligor and obligee, it is not necessary that notice of its performance should be given to the obligor. (u) So also if the obligor is bound to take notice at his peril, as if the condition be to pay the obligee a sum of money on the day of his marriage, or to indemnify against the acts of a third person, or to stand to an award, a notice is unnecessary. Performance: If the condition be for the performance of an act by act which at the time of the execution of the bond was possible, but which afterwards became impossible by the act of God, the obligor will be excused. As if the condition be to appear in Court on a certain day, and the obligor die before the day, or if A. bind himself apprentice to the obligor for seven years, and the latter enters into a bond conditioned to pay A., his executors, or administrators, a sum of money at the end or determination of his apprenticeship, and A. dies at the end of six years; in these and the like cases the bond is discharged. (z)

The cases are somewhat contradictory as to whether the obligation is discharged when the condition consists of two parts, and one becomes impossible by the act of God. But it seems that if the condition be in the disjunctive, as to grant a lease to the obligee for life by a certain day, or pay him 100l., if the obligee die before the day appointed, his executor is entitled to the 100l. (a) And where the condition was that an intended marriage between A. and B. should take effect, and if B. survived A. and did not receive 300l. of A. by his will or by the custom of London, within three months after the death of A., that then if the obligor paid to B. or her executors, 500l. within six months after, the bond should be void, and afterwards the marriage takes effect, and B. survives A. and dies within three months without receiving any part of the 300l. by the

(+) Bac. Ab. Condition, N. & (4) Anonymeus, 1 Salk. 170, conQ.; Shep. Touch. 372; Thomas v. tra; Laughter's case, 5 Co. 22, S. C.

PERFORMANCE OR BREACH OF CONDITION.

Will of A. or by the custom of London; it seems the death of B. within three months would not excuse the obligor from paying 500/. to the executors of B. (4)

The obligation may also be discharged by the act of the obligee; as if the condition be for payment of money, and the obligee refuses it when tendered; (c) or if it be for the performance of a collateral act which is rendered impossible or prevented by the obligee, as a condition to marry a certain person before a particular day, and before that day the obligee himself marries her; or if the condition be for the quiet enjoyment of copyhold lands without the interruption of any, and the lord enters for a forfeiture by non-payment of rent according to the custom; or if the bond be conditioned for the payment of rent, and the obligor is evicted by the obligee: in all these cases the bond is discharged. (d)

The obligor cannot discharge himself by showing an impossibility of performance arising from his own act, but the terms of the condition may be such that performance may become impossible from the convention of the parties, in which case both obligation and condition will be void. Thus, where the obligor, upon his marriage with a widow, gave a bond conditioned to pay her children by her late husband 300/. within twelve months after her death, if upon an account taken the stock in trade and effects of the business if then carried on by the obligor should amount to 400/.; but if upon such account being taken, the stock in trade should amount to less than 400/., then the obligor should pay to such children 120/. The obligor having in the lifetime of his wife discontinued the trade and ceased to have any stock, the obligation was held to be discharged. (¢)

When stamped.

Progressive duty.

Bonds for payment of a sum certain,

(50)

CHAPTER III.

OF THE STAMP.

—

Before a bond can be received in evidence, it must have a stamp of the proper value. It is not, however, necessary that this should be done at the time of its execution, but it may be stamped at any period upon payment of the penalty of 5/ unless the bond requires a progressive duty, in which case the penalty is 10/ (a).

The addition of another obligor after the execution of the bond, but before it has been accepted by the obligee, will not render a fresh stamp necessary; (b) and though several persons are bound for the performance of certain acts by each and every of them, yet the whole being one transaction a single stamp is sufficient. (c)

By the schedule of the 55 G. 3, c. 184, bonds are arranged under several distinct heads, and the amount of the stamp varies accordingly. (d) But in addition a progressive duty of five-and-thirty shillings is required for every 1080 words above the first 1080.

Ist. Bonds given as a security for the payment of a definite and certain sum of money, which require a stamp varying from one to twenty-five pounds, according to the amount of the sum secured. (d)

A bond given for the quarterly payment of an annual rent is a bond for the payment of a definite and certain sum of money. (e)

(a) Sand. Pl. & Ev. 287; 274; and see Goodson v. Forbes, 6 M.& S. Taunt. 175.
(b) Matson v. Booth, 6 M.& S. Taunt. 175; Stead v. Liddard, 1223, and see Spicer v. Burgess, 1 Bing. 196.
(c) C. M.& R. 129.
(d) See appendix.
(e) Bowen v. Ashley, 1 N. Rep.

### --- Page 0074 ---

OF THE STAMP.

sum, though payable at future periods, and therefore falls within this branch of the schedule, (d)

2d. Bonds given as a security for the repayment of money to be thereafter advanced, either with or without any sum already advanced; and if the sum secured by the bond, or to be ultimately recoverable, is uncertain and without limit, a 25/ stamp is requisite; but if the sum be clearly limited, the duty is the same as on a bond for the payment of such limited amount.

In order to ascertain the amount of the stamp required, the condition of the bond, and not the penalty, must be looked to. Since the penalty will not be considered as limiting the amount of the security, without there is an express provision to that effect, (e) and therefore a bond conditioned for securing the payment of money advanced and to be advanced should be stamped as a bond for securing an unlimited sum. (f) So where the condition was to pay any sum of money advanced, not exceeding 1000/., together with all such lawful charges and allowances as are usually made by bankers, it was held that a 5/ stamp was not sufficient. (g) But where the sum to be advanced is clearly limited; as where the condition expressly stipulates that the money to be ultimately recoverable shall not exceed a certain sum, an ad valorem stamp is sufficient. (h)

3d. Bonds given as a security for the transfer of stock, which require the same stamp as a bond for the payment of such limited amount. (i)

OF THE STAMP.

1. The stamp of money equal to the value of the stock on the day of the date of the bond, or on any ten days preceding.

2. Bonds for the payment of money secured by mortgage, (i) bearing even date with the bond, or for the performance of covenants contained in the mortgage deed, require only a 1/. stamp.

3. Where one and the same sum of money was secured by mortgage deed and by bond, which were executed at the same time, but did not bear the same date, and the mortgage deed was impressed with an ad valorem stamp, and the bond with a stamp of 1/.; it was held the bond was not properly stamped. (j)

4. Annuity bonds. If the bond is the principal security for the payment of the annuity upon the original creation and sale thereof, it must bear the same stamp as an actual grant or conveyance.

5. If merely a collateral security, given upon the original creation and sale of the annuity, a 1/. stamp is sufficient. Where the bond is not given at the original creation and sale of the annuity, and the money (not being interest or rent reserved upon a lease) is to be paid at stated periods, so that the total amount can be previously ascertained, the same stamp is required as bonds for the payment of a sum certain.

6. But when the total amount cannot be previously ascertained, an ad valorem duty is necessary. (/)

7. Bonds given to indemnify any person who has become surety for the payment of money or transfer of stock require a five-and-thirty shilling stamp.

8. Where principal and surety were jointly and severally bound to pay the creditors of a third person 14s. in the pound on the amount of their debts, and by the same

Indorsements of receipt of money due on bond are also exempted from receipt stamps, (s) and where such indorsements have left no blank space for receipts of subsequent payments to be written on the bond, such receipts on an unstamped piece of paper annexed to the bond are admissible in evidence. (t)

### --- Page 0078 ---

CHAPTER IV.
OF PARTICULAR BONDS.

The preceding observations are applicable to bonds in general; it is now proposed to consider such particular bonds as are most worthy of notice; and in pursuing this part of the subject the following arrangement will be observed:—

Ist. Bonds which owe their origin to some statutory enactment: this class is of considerable extent, since where a security is required by statute, it is for the most part by bond. The statute in general prescribes the form of the condition, which must be strictly adhered to; but it has been held that bonds taken by commissioners under the Tax Act (a) is not void if taken with one surety only, notwithstanding the statute directs two. (5) It would be unprofitable to enumerate every case of a bond required by statute; the present observations will therefore be confined to those particular bonds the law respecting which is more generally useful and important, viz. bail bonds, replevin bonds, administration bonds, and bastardy bonds.

2d. Bonds to the validity of which some formality is required by statute; as annuity and arbitration bonds.

3d. Particular bonds, not controlled or regulated by any provision of the legislature; as bonds to replace stock, bottomry and respondentia, post obit, and indemnity bonds.

(2) 43 Geo. 3, c. 99, and see (6) Beppin v. Cooper, 2 B. & A. Collins v. Gwyne, 2 Moore & Scott, 431.

640

### --- Page 0079 ---

Sheriffs must take bail.

To what process the statute applies.

OF BAIL BONDS.

Section 1.

Of Bail Bonds.

By the 23 Hen.6, c. 10, s. 5, it is enacted, "that sheriffs, under-sheriffs, bailiffs of franchise, and other
bailiffs, shall let out of prison all manner of persons by them or any of them arrested, or being in their custody
by force of any writ, bill, or warrant, in any action personal, or by cause of indictment of trespass, upon
reasonable sureties of sufficient persons having sufficient within the counties where such persons be so let
to bail or mainprise, to keep their days in such place as the said writs, bills, or warrants shall require; such
person or persons which be or shall be in their ward by condemnation, execution, capias utlagatum, or excommunication, surety of the peace, and all such persons which be or shall be committed to ward by special
command of any justice, and vagabonds refusing to serve according to the form of the statute of labourers
only excepted. And that no sheriff, or any of the officers or ministers aforesaid, shall take, or cause to be taken or make, any obligation for any cause aforesaid, or by color of their office, but only to themselves,
of any person, nor by any person that shall be in their ward by the course of the law, but by the name of
their office, and upon condition written that the said prisoners shall appear at the day contained in the said
writ, bill, or warrant, and in such places as the said writs, bills, or warrants shall require. And if any of the
said sheriffs, or other officers or ministers aforesaid, take any obligation in other form by color of their offices,
it shall be void."

The sheriff is not authorized by the above statute to admit to bail persons arrested by him under process.

<br>

### --- Page 0080 ---

OF BAIL BONDS.

Issuing upon an indictment at the quarter sessions. (c)
But though it was formerly considered that the sheriff could not take a bail bond for an attachment on contempt for nonpayment of costs, on the ground that such attachment was a criminal process; (d) it has been by later authorities determined that a bond taken by him in such cases is good; since, as the nonpayment of money creates a civil right, the attachment which is issued to enforce that right must be considered in the nature of civil process. (e)

It has also been formerly held that the sheriff could not take bail on an attachment out of Chancery, because the statute refers only to Courts of Common Law; it appears, however, that though such bonds are not within the provisions of the statute, nor compellable to be taken, yet that they are good at common law; and that whether or no they shall be taken is entirely in the discretion of the sheriff, as regulated by the practice of the court. (f)

The security required by the statute is a bond, and therefore any agreement not under seal is void; (g) and the bond must be made to the sheriff himself by his name of office, and not to his bailiff; for the statute applies only to those bailiffs who have the return of process; and when process is directed to the sheriff, the indemnity must be to him. (h)

There must be a condition to the bond; therefore,

If the bond be single, or with an impossible condition, as to appear at a day past; (i) or if it be executed before the condition is filled up, (j) it will be void.

And the condition should be for the defendant’s appearance at the day and in such place as is by the writ required. (k)

Since the alteration of the process for the commencement of personal actions, the terms of the condition are necessarily altered, and many of the former decisions on this subject are no longer applicable. (l)

If the security be conformable to the statute in the above respects, a variance in the description of the action will be immaterial, for it is not necessary that the bond should disclose the nature of the suit; (m) and if wrongly stated, it may be rejected as surplusage.

Where the writ issued against two defendants, and the sheriff took a bail bond conditioned for the appearance of one only, it was held no ground of demurrer. (n)

Although the statute uses the word “sureties” in the plural number, the bond will not be void if taken with one surety only. (o)

If several sureties be tendered, and two of them are respectively worth property to the amount of the penalty of the bond, it is immaterial what the others are worth. (r)

The 12 Geo. 1, c. 29, s. 2, directs that the sheriff shall take bail for the sum indorsed upon the writ, and no more. In practice, however, it is always taken in double the sum sworn to; and if it be taken in a sum above that amount, the bond will not, for that reason, be void. (s)

At common law these bonds were not assignable so as to give the plaintiff a right to sue upon them in his own name. Pleas.

What may be pleaded in a suit on a bail bond is as follows: (m) and if it be alleged that assignment was made in the presence of two credible witnesses, it is sufficient, without averring that the indorsement was attested by two credible witnesses; but if the assignment appears on the declaration to have been attested by one witness only, it will be demurrable. As the assignment is not by deed a profert is unnecessary. (n)

Formerly any matter of fact which rendered the bond void, as that it was executed after the time limited by the process for putting in special bail, or before the condition was filled up, might have been taken advantage of under the plea of non est factum; but now that plea only puts in issue the fact of the execution of the bond, and all matters which show it either voidable or void must be pleaded specially. (p)

The defendant may plead that no process issued against the principal, or that the debt was levied on the principal since the commencement of the action, or having craved oyer of the condition he may plead an appearance at the day therein mentioned. (q) and this plea should conclude with a verification by the record. (r) It may also be pleaded that the bond was not assigned according to the form of the statute, (s) or that it was taken for ease and favour after the time limited for putting in special bail; and to this plea, if the action be at the suit of the sheriff, he should pray an enrolment of the bond, and after setting it out, state

OF BAIL BONDS.

After the bond is forfeited, the giving time to the principal will not discharge the bail. (f) But if the plaintiff take a cognovit from the defendant without the knowledge of the bail, he cannot afterwards proceed upon the bail bond. (g) Also where a cognovit is taken with the consent of the bail, and the debt is not paid, the plaintiff must give the bail notice that the cognovit is unsatisfied before he can commence proceedings against them.
