SEcTION 2.
Of Replevin Bonds.

The statute Westminster 2, (13 Edw. 1,) c. 2, s. 3, provides, “that the sheriff or bailiffs from thenceforth shall not only receive the plaintiff’s pledges for the pursuing of the suit, before they make deliverance of the distress, but also for the return of the beasts, if return be awarded. And if any take pledges otherwise, he shall answer for the price of the beasts, and the lord that distraineth shall have his recovery by writ, that he shall restore unto him so many beasts or cattle; and if the bailiff be not able to restore, his superior shall restore.”

And the 1 & 2 P.& M.c. 12, s. 3, enacts, “that every sheriff shall, at his first county court day, or within

OF REPLEVIN BONDS.

Within two months next after he has received his patent of office, depute, appoint, and proclaim, in the shire or town within his bailiwick, four deputies at the least, dwelling not above twelve miles one distant from another, which said deputies so appointed and proclaimed, shall have authority in the sheriff’s name to make replevies and deliverance of distresses in such manner and form as the sheriff may and ought to do.”

Under the statute of Westminster, the sheriff might, according to his discretion, have taken a bond either from the plaintiff, or from one or more sureties; (i) the practice under that statute has however been, in some measure, superseded by the provisions of the 11 Geo. 2, c. 19, s. 23, which enacts, “that all sheriffs and other officers, having authority to grant replevins, may and shall, in every replevin of a distress for rent, take, in their own names, from the plaintiff and two responsible persons as sureties, a bond in double the value of the goods distrained, (such value to be ascertained by the oath of one or more credible witnesses,) and conditioned for prosecuting the suit with effect and without delay, and for duly returning the goods and chattels distrained in case a return shall be awarded, before any deliverance be made of the distress; and that such sheriff, or other officer as aforesaid, taking any such bond, shall, at the request and costs of the avowant, or person making cognizance, assign such bond to the avowant or person aforesaid, by indorsing the same, and attesting it under his hand and seal by two or more credible witnesses; (k) and if the bond so taken and assigned be forfeited, the avowant, or person

Sureties.

OF REPLEVIN BONDS,

making cognizance, may bring an action and recover thereupon in his own name, and the Court where such action shall be brought may, by a rule of the same Court, give relief to the parties upon such bond as may be agreeable to justice and reason, and such rule shall have the nature and effect of a defeazance to such bond.”

Though by the language of this statute it is imperative on the sheriff to take a bond, the Court will not grant an attachment against him in case of neglecting so to do; (Z) nor will they order him to pay the costs recovered by the defendant in replevin, but the proper remedy is to proceed against him by an action on the case. (m)

It is not necessarily incumbent on the sheriff to inquire narrowly into the circumstances and responsibility of the persons taken as sureties; (n) it is sufficient if they are apparently, though not actually so; (o) but if it appear that the sheriff had notice of their insufficiency, or neglected the means of information within his power, and did not act under the circumstances, and considering the information he received with a reasonable degree of caution, he will be liable, if either of the sureties be insufficient. (y) The general reputation as to want of credit of the sureties in the neighbourhood is evidence against him. (g) It seems that where the sureties reside out of the bailiwick of the sheriff, see Yea v. Lethbridge, 4 T. R. 433; Concanen v. Lethbridge, 2 H. Bl. 36; Evans v. Brander, 2 H. Bl. 547; Baker v. (p) Scott v. Waithman, 3 Stark. 368.

The sheriff is not bound to pursue strictly the terms of 11 Geo. 2, c. 19, s. 23. If the bond be conditioned to prosecute with effect, it will be good, although it do not require that the suit shall be prosecuted without delay, and although it contains an undertaking to indemnify the sheriff. (s) So if the bond be executed by one surety only, it will be available by the sheriff against such surety; for the 11 Geo. 2, c. 19, has not avoided the power which the sheriff formerly possessed under the statute of Westminster. But where the person making cognizance sued the sheriff upon a bond with one surety only, for taking insufficient pledges, and recovered damages and costs, and the sheriff afterwards sued the surety upon the bond for not having returned the goods; it was held the sheriff was not entitled to recover the costs of the action against him as such sheriff; and that as the surety was deprived of calling on his co-surety for contribution, he was only liable to a moiety of the damages awarded by the jury in an action against the sheriff. (t)

and independent of each other, and a breach of any one of them will occasion a forfeiture. (2)

The term prosecuting a suit with effect means with success, and extends to one continued prosecution from the commencement until the termination of the suit, whether in the County Court or the Court above. Thus, where to debt on bond the defendant pleaded that he had prosecuted the suit with effect in the County Court, but that a writ of error had been brought in the Court above, where the judgment was reversed; and the plaintiff replied that the judgment in the Court above also was that the plaint in the Court below should abate, and that there should be a return irrepleviable; upon demurrer to this replication, the Court held that the words "to prosecute the suit with effect" in the Court below, were not confined to the prosecution in that Court only, but extended also to the prosecution of the writ of error, as that was part of the suit commenced below. (y) So where the plaint is removed by re. fa. lo. into a superior Court, the condition of the bond is not satisfied by having prosecuted the suit with effect in the County Court, but that the plaintiff must also follow it into the Court above. (z)

But if the plaintiff’s neglect to prosecute the suit has been occasioned by the act of the defendant, as by his not appearing to the summons, (a) or if the proceedings have been stayed by injunction, and during that period the plaintiff dies, the defendant will not be entitled to an assignment of the bond. If the action be brought before the forfeiture of the bond, the court will not set aside the proceedings, but will leave the defendant to plead that fact in bar. (/)
It was formerly considered doubtful whether a bond which did not in every respect conform to the provisions of 11 Geo. 2, could be assigned; (k) but it seems to have been settled by a late decision, that if the bond be conditioned to prosecute the suit with effect, the assignee may sue upon it, though it do not in other respects follow the terms of the act. Section III.

Of Administration Bonds.

22 & 23 Car. 2, c. 10, requires the ordinary to take from the person to whom administration is granted a bond with two or more sureties, and also prescribes the form of the condition, which is in substance as follows:

1st. That the administrator make a true and perfect inventory of the goods of the intestate, and exhibit the same in the registry of the Prerogative Court.

2d. That he well and truly administer, according to law, the same, and all other goods that shall come to his hands or possession, or to the hands or possession of any other person for him.

3rd. That he make a true and just account of his said administration.

4th. That he deliver and pay over the rest and residue of the goods that shall be found remaining upon his account unto such persons respectively as the judge of the Court shall, by decree or sentence, pursuant to the above statute, limit and appoint.

5th. That he deliver the letters of administration into court, in case any will of the deceased shall appear.

The non-delivery by the administrator of a true and perfect inventory (g), or that he has not made a true and just account, may be well assigned as a breach of the condition of the bond. But the questions that have arisen upon these bonds are principally confined to the clause requiring the administrator well and truly to administer according to law; and in an early case (h),

(g) Greenside v. Bensun, 3 Atk. 252, Wills, 1 Salk. 316.

Form of condition,

Breaches.

<br>

### --- Page 0097 ---

Non-payment of debt to a creditor not a breach of condition.

Neglect of administrator to distribute surplus of intestate’s estate, before decree of ecclesiastical court, not a breach of condition.

OF ADMINISTRATION BONDS.

It was held, that the non-payment of a debt to a creditor could not be assigned as a breach of this part of the condition. The law on this subject has been fully examined and settled by late authorities; and it is clearly established that the neglect of an administrator to distribute the surplus of the intestate’s effects before a decree of the Ecclesiastical Court has directed him so to do, cannot be assigned as a breach of the condition of the bond. (2)

To debt on bond, with condition as above, the defendant, after craving oyer of the bond and condition, pleaded, that the administrator had performed the three first branches of the condition; and that as to the fourth branch, that the judge of the Court had not, by decree or sentence, limited or appointed the administrator to pay the residue of the goods, or any part thereof, which were found remaining upon the said accounts of the administrator to any person whatever; but that, on the contrary, the administrator was cited to appear before the commissary of the Court to exhibit an inventory, render an account of his administration, and see portions allotted, and a distribution made of the goods; that the administrator did appear in consequence of such citation, and such proceedings were had in the court, that he was duly dismissed from all further observance of justice in the cause. To this plea the plaintiff by his replication alleged and assigned, as a breach of the condition, that certain persons particularly named were the only next of kin of the intestate; that the administrator paid all her debts; that goods of great value, and more than sufficient to pay all debts and charges of the administration, came to the hands of the administrator; and that he did not distribute the surplus as directed by the court. (1) Archbishop of Canterbury v. Tappen, 8 B. & C. 151.

<br>

### --- Page 0098 ---

OF ADMINISTRATION BONDS.

Of the administrator, which ought, according to the condition, to have been duly administered by him according to law, that is to say, in manner following, to wit, by paying certain sums (specified in the replication) to the persons before-mentioned as the next of kin, whereof the administrator had notice; that a reasonable time for doing this had elapsed, yet the administrator had not administered the goods according to law, or paid or delivered the goods, or any part thereof, to the persons before-named, or either of them, but neglected and refused so to do, contrary to the effect of the condition; whereby the persons before-named have lost the use and profit of their proportions of the goods. The defendant having rejoined, that the judge of the Court had not by decree or sentence limited and appointed the administrator to deliver or pay the goods to the persons named, or any other person; the plaintiff demurred generally, and the point for argument was, whether the refusal of the administrator to distribute the surplus or residue of the effects of the intestate among the next of kin according to the statute of distributions, without the previous decree or sentence of the Court, was a breach of the condition of the bond. Lord Tenterden, C. J., after commenting upon the statute and the terms of the condition, observed, “that the words ‘well and truly administer the goods according to law’ could not be understood of an administration comprising a distribution among the next of kin; that where an administrator intends to act faithfully, and the claims of the next of kin can (as in general they may be) ascertained without difficulty, he will not put them to the expense and delay of calling for his account, and obtaining the sentence of a court; and therefore it is well said that it is his duty to administer the goods according to law, and not to distribute them among the next of kin.”

<br>

### --- Page 0099 ---

Conversion of effects by administrator a breach of condition.

OF ADMINISTRATION BONDS.

But where an administrator converts the effects of the intestate to his own use, and afterwards becomes bankrupt, and the effects are thereby wholly lost, such conduct will be a direct breach of that part of the condition requiring him well and truly to administer the goods of the intestate, and the surety in the bond will be liable for the full amount of the money so misapplied.

To debt against the defendant as surety on a joint and several administration bond, he pleaded non est factum; and the plaintiff assigned breaches on the roll negativing the several branches of the condition. On the trial it appeared that the administrator had possessed himself of the intestate’s effects to a considerable amount, one part of which he applied in discharge of the debts and funeral expenses of the intestate, another part in payment of one of the next of kin; but the residue he converted to his own use, and afterwards becoming bankrupt it was entirely lost to the estate of the intestate. A verdict was found for the plaintiff, subject to the opinion of the Court as to whether the conduct of the administrator amounted to a breach of that part of the condition which required him well and truly to administer the effects of the intestate, and the court were of opinion that, looking at the language of the bond, nothing could be a more direct infringement of the terms of the condition than the application by the administrator of the effects of the intestate to his own use, and the converting them to his

Section 4.

Of Bastardy Bonds.

The Poor Law Amendment Act (m) has repealed the statutory provisions under which bastardy bonds were formerly given. Before that act came into operation, magistrates were empowered to commit to prison any person charged with being the father of a bastard child, until he should give security to indemnify the parish. (n) Now, however, when any bastard child has become actually chargeable to the parish, the overseers or guardians may, after diligent enquiry as to the father of the child, apply to the quarter sessions for an order on the putative father to reimburse the parish; and if the Court, after hearing evidence thereon, shall be satisfied that the person charged is really and in truth the father of the child, it shall make such order as shall appear reasonable under the circumstances of the case. (0)

The 70th section of 4 & 5 Wm. 4, c. 76, enacts, “that every security given, or recognizance entered into, by any person or persons, or his or her surety, before the

OF BASTARDY BONDS.

Passing of that act, to indemnify any parish or place as to any child or children likely to be born a bastard or bastards, whereof any single woman shall be pregnant at the time of the passing of that act, or to abide and perform such order or orders as might have been made touching such child or children, pursuant to an Act made and passed in the 18th year of the reign of her late Majesty Queen Elizabeth, concerning bastards begotten and born out of lawful matrimony, shall be and the same are thereby declared null and void; and every person who shall, at the time of the passing of that act, be in custody upon the commitment of any justice or justices for not having given such security, or entered into such recognizance, shall be discharged (upon the application of such person) by any one of the visiting justices of the gaol in which such person shall be in custody under any such commitment.”

As, however, the 4 & 5 Wm. 4, c. 76, only applies to future born bastards, and does not affect bonds already entered into for indemnifying parishes against the expense of children born before the passing of that act, it is considered expedient briefly to notice the law respecting such bonds.

Where the bond was given under compulsion of the statute, its provisions must have been strictly complied with, and therefore the condition could only be to indemnify the parish. (p) But if a party voluntarily gave a bond to the parish officers for the purpose of providing for the maintenance of his illegitimate offspring, such bond was valid, even though it went beyond an indemnity to the parish. (q) The right of

(p), Cole v. Gower, 6 East, 110.

350. So at the present time a

<br>

### --- Page 0102 ---

OF BASTARDY BONDS.

Suing upon the bond vested in the overseers for the time being; and as they were under a legal obligation to maintain illegitimate children, it was not necessary that there should have been an order made for that purpose, to enable them to recover on the bond. They could only recover the sum actually expended in the maintenance of the child. And in no case was the obligor liable beyond the amount of the penalty.

It has been frequently mooted, though never yet determined, whether or no a putative father has a right to the custody of his illegitimate child, and can plead his willingness to maintain it, in discharge of an action on the bond. In a late case such a plea was put upon the record; but the court there held, that as the bond was not in terms conditioned to indemnify the parish, but was simply for the performance of one particular act, namely, the payment of a weekly sum on account of costs, charges, and expenses; it was not necessary to enter into the question as to how far the offer of the father to maintain the child would relieve him from his liability.

This point is still of some importance, since, if such a defence could be pleaded to a bond, it is equally applicable to the enforcement of an order made by the quarter sessions, in pursuance of the provisions of the recent act.

Section 5.

Of Annuity Bonds.

By the 53 Geo. 3, c. 141, (which repealed the 17 Geo. 3, c. 26, except so far as regards annuities granted before the passing of the first mentioned act,) it is required “that within thirty days after the execution of every bond, ( &c.) whereby any annuity or rent charge is granted for one or more life or lives, or for any term of years or greater estate determinable on one or more life or lives, a memorial of the date of every such bond, of the names of all the parties, and of all the witnesses thereto, and of the person or persons for whose life or lives such annuity or rent charge shall be granted, and of the person or persons by whom the same is to be beneficially received, the pecuniary consideration or considerations for granting the same, and the annual sum or sums to be paid, shall be enrolled in the High Court of Chancery, otherwise every such bond shall be null and void to all intents and purposes.”

By the 4th section it is enacted, that where the persons to whom the annuity is granted shall not be entitled beneficially thereto, the names of the persons who are so entitled shall be described in the enrolment, otherwise the bond shall be null and void.

The 10th section provides that the act shall not extend:—

Ist. To any annuity or rent charge secured upon freehold lands of equal or greater value than the annuity, over and above any other annuity charged or secured for such lands.

2. To any voluntary annuity or rent charge granted

<br>

### --- Page 0104 ---

OF ANNUITY BONDS.

Without regard to pecuniary consideration, or money’s worth.

3rd. To any annuity granted by any body corporate, or under any authority or trust created by act of parliament.

To constitute an annuity within the meaning of the above act, the principal must be irrecoverably gone; therefore a bond conditioned for the payment of a sum of money to the executors of the obligee, and interest in the meantime to him, is not an annuity bond, and does not require to be enrolled. (2)

To bring an annuity within the first part of the 10th section, the “other annuity” must be directly and specifically charged on the land, and not merely secured in a manner that may by possibility affect it, as by judgment entered up on a warrant of attorney. (a)

This statute applies only to annuities for which a pecuniary consideration has been given. Thus where the condition of the bond recited that the plaintiff was entitled to an interest in certain mines of coals for life, and that by indentures of even date with the bond the annuitant assigned such interest to the defendants, who in consideration thereof had agreed to pay her a certain annuity for life, and the bond was conditioned for the payment of that annuity; the defendants, having pleaded that no memorial was enrolled, the Court, in giving judgment, observed, that the act did not extend to cases of fair and bond fide sales of landed property, whether freehold, for life, or leasehold, where the consideration in part or in whole might be an annuity to be paid to the vendor. (b)

(s) Winter v. Mouseley, 2 B.& A. Adol. 315.
(806.) (b) James v. James, 5 Moore.

(2) Walford v. Marchant, 2 B. & A. 479.

81

What annuities are within the above act.
What annuity is within the first part of the 10th section.
Annuities not founded on a pecuniary consideration need not be enrolled.

### --- Page 0105 ---

To require enrolment there must be an actual sale of an annuity for money, bills, or goods.

So where by the condition of the bond it appeared that £10,000 was to be given by a father on the marriage of his daughter, to trustees upon trust to pay the income, interest to the husband for life, and the father died with goods. out having paid the principal to trustees, and his affairs being embarrassed, and it being uncertain whether there would be sufficient to pay his debts, the husband agreed to accept, in lieu of the £10,000, £6,000 and an annuity of £125 payable during his life; it was held, that though the preamble of the 53 Geo. 3, c. 14, was not so extensive in terms as the 17 Geo. 3, c. 26, yet there was sufficient in other parts of the first-mentioned act to show that the legislature had in view the same object, viz. the preventing the sale of life annuities; and that to bring a case within the 53 Geo. 3, c. 14, there must be an actual sale of an annuity for money, bills, or goods.

Again, where the condition of the bond recited, that the defendant having been indebted to the late husband of the plaintiff in a large sum of money on a balance of accounts between them, and it was agreed that, in lieu of payment of part of such balance, the defendant should by his bond secure to the plaintiff, after the decease of her husband, during the joint lives of the plaintiff and defendant, an annuity which was stated in the husband’s will to be settled upon the plaintiff; that the husband was dead, and that the annuity had been paid up to a certain period; the condition therefore was, that the defendant should, during

OF ANNUITY BONDS.

Under the 17 Geo. 3, c. 26, frequent questions arose as to what was a sufficient compliance with that act, in the statement of the nature and extent of the securities, (f) and the description of the consideration. (g)
The 53 Geo. 3, c. 141, in some measure obviated the previous uncertainty, by providing a concise tabular form of the statement of the memorial. (h) From this form, however, it became questionable whether or no it was necessary to state the description and place of residence of the witnesses to the bond, as the enacting clause of the statute did not require it. The Court of King’s Bench were of opinion that it was the intention of the legislature that they should be stated; and where a witness was merely described as the clerk of an attorney whose abode was mentioned, they considered the description as insufficient, as the clerk did not reside there, but merely attended at the office. (i)
Considerable doubt having been entertained as to whether the right construction had been put upon this act, (j) the question was set at rest by 3 Geo. 4, c. 92, which enacted that no further or other description of the subscribing witness to any bond, whereby any annuity or rent-charge is granted, is required in the

Plea of no memorial.

OF ANNUITY BONDS.

memorial, besides the names of all such witnesses.
The second section declares that every bond granting any annuity, of which a memorial shall have been enrolled, shall be valid, notwithstanding the omission to enrol any other bond for any such annuity.

The 7 Geo. 4, c. 75, further provides that the names of the witnesses may be stated in the memorial as they appear in the bond.

Where the defendant relies upon the want of enrolment, and the consideration does not appear by the bond itself, it is not enough to plead generally that no memorial of it was enrolled, but it must likewise be shown that the consideration was pecuniary. (k)

And if it be pleaded that the memorial is insufficient, it is necessary to show in what way it is defective, or that no other memorial has been enrolled. (m)

And where to an action of debt on bond the defendant pleaded no such memorial as the statute requires, to which the plaintiff replied that there was a memorial, which contained the names of the parties, and the consideration for which the annuity was granted; the defendant rejoined that the consideration was untruly alleged in the memorial to have been paid to both obligors, for that one of them did not receive any part of it: the rejoinder was held bad; first, because it was a departure from the plea; secondly, because the fact alleged did not contradict the replication, for the consideration might have been paid to the other obligor on account of himself and the co-obligor, or to a stranger for both of them. (m)

SECTIon 6.

Of Arbitration Bonds.

Where matters in difference are referred to arbitration, the submission is frequently made by cross bonds.

In such case the obligatory part is in the form of a common money bond, with condition to abide the event of the award.

The 9 & 10 Will. 3, c.15, s. 1, enables the parties to insert in the condition a stipulation that their submission shall be made a rule of a Court of Record. (y) The condition should also contain such powers, provisoes, and stipulations as are usually inserted when the reference is by agreement or indenture. These will of course vary according to the nature of each particular case; but as a minute inquiry into this subject would be foreign to a treatise of this description, the reader is referred, for information on this point, to the several works on arbitration. (7)

It should, however, be observed, that in every case the condition ought to contain a stipulation against revocation by death, marriage, or bankruptcy. Where there were three obligors, and one died before award made, it was held that the arbitrator could not award (0) Wilson v. Mushett, 3 B. & Adol. 752. (q) See particularly Chit. Gen. Prac., p. 73, where the subject is fully treated.

(r) But where the deed of submission provided that the arbitrator’s authority should not expire through the death of either party, and the arbitrator by his award directed the executors of one of the parties, who died before award made, to pay a certain sum to the other; and the party in whose favour the award was made, sued the surety on the bond, in which the clause as to the death was not inserted, and the surety pleaded performance according to the covenants contained in the deed of submission; it was held that the authority of the arbitrator was not determined by the death. (s)

An executor should stipulate against being personally liable, otherwise he admits assets by entering into the bond. (t)

Impartial or improper conduct in an arbitrator in making his award, cannot be pleaded in bar to an action on the bond, but is only matter for application to the equitable jurisdiction of the Court. ()

Section 7.

Of Bonds to replace Stock.

Bonds to replace a loan of stock on a certain day, and in the meantime to pay the dividends, are not of themselves illegal, since there is a possibility of a depreciation. (r) Edmunds v. Cor, 2 Chit. Rep. Riddell v. Sutton, 5 Bing. 200; 432, Wansborough v. Dyer, 2 Chit. 40.
(s) Macdougall v. Robertson, (in (u) Braddick v. Thompson, 8
error,) 2 Y.& J. 5.11; 1 M.& P. 147- East, S445 Grasebrook v. Davis, 5
(t) Barry v. Rush, 1 T.R. 691% BL & C. 534.

<br>

### --- Page 0110 ---

BONDS TO REPLACE STOCK.

In the value of the stock: (a) but if the agreement be such that the lender is secure from loss, and receives, by way of dividend, more than the legal rate of interest, the contract may be avoided on the ground of usury. Thus, where it appeared that the obligor was indebted to the obligee in £486: 4s. 6d., which the former being unable to pay, it was agreed that the latter should forbear an action which he had commenced until a certain day, in consideration of the obligor giving to him a bond to transfer on that day so much stock as £486: 4s. 6d. would purchase at the then present day's price, which would amount to £908: 16s. 7d., together with such interest as the sum would have produced as such stock in the meantime. The jury having found the transaction fair, and not a mere colour for usury, the Court agreed the bond was legal, for the amount of the sum to be paid by the obligor depended on a contingency, and if the stocks had greatly fallen in the meantime, the obligee might have received less than his principal and interest. (v) So where the condition was, that the obligors should, on a certain day, transfer to the obligee £400 stock, or pay to him the value thereof, and it appeared that the value of the stock, when the agreement was made, was £240, but at the time of the action brought £225 only, this bond was adjudged to be legal. (z) Also it has been held, that a bond, with condition for the re-transfer of stock on a certain day, and in default thereof for payment of the money produced by the sale, and in the meantime also for payment of the dividends, is not usurious, even

Measure of damages,

BONDS TO REPLACE STOCK.

Though the dividends exceed the legal rate of interest. (a) :

But where there was a contract for the repayment of
a debt, or at the option of the creditor, to transfer so
much stock as the amount of the debt would have produced on the day it was payable, the transaction was
considered usurious; for the principal and interest
being secured, the creditor might take the opportunity
of a rise in the stocks to make his election, and thus he
would be sure to be a gainer, while he could not possibly sustain any loss. (b) And in every case where the
exact value of the stock to be sold or replaced has been
previously estimated by the parties, so that nothing depends upon contingency, the contract will be usurious
if more than the legal rate of interest is reserved. (c)

In an action for a breach of the condition to replace
stock, the true measure of damages appears to be
its value at the day appointed for the transfer, if the
stock have fallen in value before the trial; (d) but if
the stock have risen in the meantime, the highest
value as it stood at the time of trial. (e) The obligee
cannot, however, recover special damage for a profit
which he might have made of it, if sooner replaced, unless he has demanded payment for that express purpose, and has given the obligor notice that he should
call on him to pay the difference. (f)

Section 8.

Of Bottomry and Respondentia Bonds.

Bottomry is an agreement by which the owner or master of a ship or vessel, in consideration of a sum of money advanced to enable him to carry on a voyage, undertakes to repay the same with a stipulated interest, if the voyage prove successful; and in the meantime pledges himself, the ship, and tackle, for the due performance of the agreement; and if the ship and tackle arrive in safety, they are liable, as well as the person of the borrower; but if the ship be lost, the lender also loses his whole money. (g)

It should be observed, that it is the essence of bottomry that the lender should take upon himself the risk and perils of the voyage; and therefore where the master of a ship in a foreign port, by an instrument under seal, bound himself, the ship, freight and cargo, for the repayment of money advanced for her repairs, with £12 per cent. bottomry premium, in eight days after his arrival in London, and the vessel, freight and cargo were to be liable, whether she arrived there or not, this was held to be misdescribed as a bottomry contract. (A)

It is not however necessary that the bond should expressly declare that the lender takes upon himself the risk of the voyage, though it is usually done; it is sufficient if that fact can be collected from the general terms of the instrument. Thus, where a bond executed

(g) Black. Com. 457; Sel. N. (h) Simonds v. Hodgson, 3 M. & P. 1037; and see 3M. & P. 385, =P. 385.

Bottomry.

Bond need not
expressly declare that the
lender takes
upon himself
the risk of the
voyage.

### --- Page 0113 ---

Respondentia.

OF BOTTOMRY AND RESPONDENTIA BONDS.

In a foreign port by the master of a ship, after reciting that his vessel bound to London had received considerable damage, and that he had borrowed a sum of money to defray the expenses of repairing her, proceeded thus:—I bind myself, my ship, her apparel, tackle, (&c.) as well as her freight and cargo, to pay the above sum, with £12 per cent. bottomry premium; and I further bind myself and the said ship, her freight and cargo, to the payment of that sum, with all charges thereon, in eight days after my arrival at the port of London; and I do hereby make liable the said vessel, her freight and cargo, whether she do or do not arrive at the port of London, in preference to all other debts or claims, declaring that this pledge or bottomry has now and must have preference to all other claims and charges, until such principal sum, with £12 per cent. bottomry premium, and all charges, are duly paid. It was held upon error from the Common Pleas, that this was an instrument of bottomry; for an intention sufficiently appeared from the whole of it, that the lender should take upon himself the peril of the voyage, and that the words my arrival, must be understood to mean my ship’s arrival; and that the words, I make liable the said vessel, her freight and cargo, whether she do or do not arrive at London, were intended only to give the lenders a claim on the ship in preference to other claims, in case of the ship’s arrival at some other than the destined port, and not to provide for the event of a loss of the ship. (2)

Where the loan is not made upon the ship or vessel, but upon the goods and merchandise which must be necessarily sold or exchanged in the course of the voyage, the instrument is called a respondentia bond. (i) Simonds v. Hodgson, 3 B. & Adol. 50; 6 Bing. 114.

<br>

### --- Page 0114 ---

OF BOTTOMRY AND RESPONDENTIA BONDS.

voyage, there the borrower only is personally bound,
and is said to take up money at respondentia. (k)

The condition of a bond, after reciting that money was lent upon the goods laden and to be laden on board a certain ship on her voyage out and home, was, that if the ship should proceed on her voyage and return within thirty-six months (the dangers of the seas excepted,) and if the borrower, within thirty-days after her arrival, should pay to the lender the sum agreed on; or if in the voyage and within the thirty-six months the ship should be lost by fire, enemies, or other casualties, the borrower should, within six months after such loss, pay to the lender a proportionable average on all the goods carried out and acquired during the voyage which should be saved, then the obligation to be void. This was held to be no more than a personal obligation from the borrower to the lender, and did not give the latter any specific pledge or lien on the home cargo, or the proceeds thereof. (/)

However much the interest reserved by these bonds exceeds the rate allowed by law, the contract will not be considered usurious, since the lender risks both principal and interest; but should the hazard go only to the interest or premium, and not to the principal also, the bond will be illegal. (m)

Nothing short of a total destruction of the ship will discharge the borrower of money upon a bottomry bond; if the ship be captured and detained for a short time, or if it exist in specie, though in such a state as might have made it prudent for the owner to dispose of it, the obligee may recover on the bond. (n)

(k) Black. Com. 457; Sel. N. S.C. 1 Lev. 54.

Section 9.

Of Post Obit Bonds.

Post obit bonds are of two kinds: Ist, When the payment of a greater sum than that advanced depends on a contingency; 2nd, When the payment of the greater sum is certain; but the time of payment is contingent. If these bonds are given on a bond fide contingency, they cannot be impeached on the ground of usury. (0) They are, however, considered as securities of a questionable nature, (p) and therefore the Courts of Law have refused to allow judgment to be entered up on a warrant of attorney, given with a post obit bond, without a rule to show cause. (g) But if the consideration be inadequate, a Court of Equity will in general grant relief, and especially in the case of expectant heirs, (r) but the obligor will be required to restore to the obligee the money paid, together with interest. (s) And as the validity of these bonds entirely depends upon the risk of the money lent, if the obligee be indemnified by an insurance on the life on which contingency depends, and the obligor bear any part of that expense, a Court of Equity will order the bond to be cancelled. (t)

(0) Chesterfield v. Janssen, 2 Ves. 17, 142, 24; Pocock v. Evans, 16 Ves. 514.
(p) Lushington v. Waller, 1 H. Bl. 94; Vainee’s case, 2 Freem. 65.
(q) Evans v. Chesshire, Belt's Sup: 311.
(r) Vainee’s case, 2 Freem. 63.

OF INDEMNITY BONDS.

Section 10.

Of Indemnity Bonds.

When the obligor of an indemnity bond undertakes
generally to save harmless from the consequences of a
particular act, the bond is forfeited upon the obligee
being damnified; and it is not necessary that notice
should be given to the obligor, whether the indemnity
be against the acts of the obligee or a third person. (1)

A condition to save harmless from all actions, legal
proceedings, and costs, which might arise from the
obligee delivering to the obligor a bill of exchange,
part of the proceeds of which a third person was entitled to, is broken by the obligee paying to such third
person his share of the proceeds upon his demanding
the same, without bringing any action. (2)

So an obligation to save harmless from all suits,
quarrels, and demands, concerning a bond in which the
party indemnified was bound as surety, is forfeited by
such surety paying the money due on the bond without
demand; (3) for where there is a legal obligation to do
the act against the consequences of which the indemnity
is given, the liability of the obligor will attach, though
that act has been done by the obligee without compulsion.

An undertaking to indemnify against a certain engagement will extend to any expense which falls upon
the obligee by reason of that engagement. (4)

And the obligor of a bond conditioned to indemnify
the obligee against his wife’s debts, is liable for the

Continuing guaranty,

Non damnificatus.

A condition to indemnify against all mortgages, judgments, and executions, had or obtained, or thereafter

confined to a lawful entry or eviction. (a)

Where the indemnity is given as a security for money
to be advanced, it frequently becomes questionable whether the guaranty is continuing. This will, of course,
depend upon the language of the instrument, and the
intent of the parties to be collected therefrom.

A condition for payment of all sums not exceeding
3000/. which should at any time thereafter be advanced
by the obligees to a third person, has been held not a
continuing guaranty, but only a guaranty for advances
once made to the extent of 3000/. (b)

But where the condition was to indemnify the obligees for such sums as they in their banking business
should for ten years advance or pay on account of bills
of exchange which a third person should, from time to
time, draw on them and make payable at their house,
and also all allowances for advancing and paying such
bills and accommodations, not exceeding in the whole
5000/.: it was held to guaranty running accounts, and
not satisfied by the part payment of 5000/. (c)

To an action on an indemnity bond, the proper plea
is non damnificatus ; and the plaintiff replies, showing

### --- Page 0119 ---

Joint.

CHAPTER V.

OF JOINT AND SEVERAL BONDS.

Two or more persons may bind themselves jointly or severally, or both jointly and severally, according to the language of the bond; but a person cannot be bound to several persons severally; and therefore, if an obligation be made to two persons in the sum of 200/., one hundred pounds to be paid to one of them, and the other hundred pounds to the other, the part as to the payment is void. (a)

If the obligation be joint, it will be attended with all the consequences of a joint contract. Thus, in suing upon the bond, all the obligors must be joined, unless some of them were incapable of executing a valid bond, as in the case of a married woman, or an infant. The omission, however, of a joint obligee is not a ground of demurrer, unless the defect clearly appears on the face of the declaration, but in other cases it should be pleaded in abatement. (b) So, also, all the obligees must join in the suit, and upon the death of one of them, the survivors alone can sue thereon, even though the bond is conditioned for payment to the deceased party, and the survivors have no interest in the sum contained in the condition. (c)

In like manner, upon the death of one of several obligors, the survivors alone can sue, unless the bond provides otherwise. (d)

(a) Bac. Ab. Oblig. (D) 3, and South v. Tanner, 2 Taunt, 254.
(b) Yelv. 177.
(c) 1 Was. Saund. 291 b, un.
(d) 1 Ves. Jr. 608.

<br>

### --- Page 0120 ---

OF JOINT AND SEVERAL BONDS.

Joint obligors, the action should be brought against the survivor, and the executor of the deceased cannot be joined. (d)

A release by one of several obligees will operate as a bar to all; (e) so, a release to one of several obligors may be pleaded in bar by the others. (/)

If the bond be several, the liability of each obligor is separate and distinct, and therefore the tearing off the seal of one of them will not avoid the bond as to the rest. (g)

Where three persons entered into a bond for the payment of 1000/. and the words used were “for which payment we bind ourselves, and each of us for himself, for the whole and entire sum of 1000/. each,” the word “each” was held to render the bond several only. (4)

If two or more persons bind themselves, and “each of them,” the obligation is joint and several. Also where a bond executed by three persons commenced with the words, “know all men, that we are bound,” &c., but proceeded, “for payment whereof I bind myself, my heirs, executors, and administrators, by these presents. Sealed with our seals.” This was considered to be both a joint and several obligation. (é)

And in this case the obligee may sue all jointly, or any one of them, at his election; and if he sue them separately, the recovery against one will be no bar to his action against the others. (h)

Where three persons entered into a bond for the payment of 1000/. and the words used were “for which payment we bind ourselves, and each of us for himself, for the whole and entire sum of 1000/. each,” the word “each” was held to render the bond several only. (4)

OF JOINT AND SEVERAL BONDS.

A discharge of one joint and several obligor will operate as a discharge of all. (k) And where a release (f) has been given to one of several obligors, the obligee cannot plead that it was given upon an express understanding that it should not prejudice his right or remedy against the others, for the legal effect of an instrument under seal cannot be altered by parol agreement. (m)

So, if several are jointly and severally bound, and the obligee make one of them his executor, and he administers, this will operate as a release of the debt at law, (n) for a personal action once suspended by the voluntary act of the party entitled to it, is gone forever. (o) And the same consequence will follow whether the obligor be sole executor, or with others. (p)

Also if the obligee make the wife of one of two joint and several obligors his executor, this will operate as a release of the debt. (q) But if the obligee make the executor of one of the obligors his executor, the debt is not discharged, for the executor holds it in another right, and he may sue the surviving obligor. (r) And where there is a debt on bond by executor of obligee against executor of obligor, the latter pleaded that the money mentioned in the condition was part of the personal estate of 4 B. deceased, by whom it had been bequeathed to the testator of the plaintiff and the

If one of several persons jointly and severally bound make the obligee executor, and the latter has assets and administers, the debt will be released in law. (s) So also if one of the obligors make the executor of the obligee his executor and leave assets, the debt is deemed satisfied. and died, and she after her husband’s death sued as his executrix, the action was held to be well brought, for by suing as executrix of her husband she waived her right to survivorship as joint obligee. (z)

It should be observed that the principle of the above doctrine is, that a remedy suspended by the voluntary act of the party is gone for ever; therefore if the suspension be caused by the act of law, there will be merely a temporary privation. (2) Thus if the obligor take out administration to the obligee and die, the administrator de bonis non of the obligee may, nevertheless, maintain an action for the debt against the executor of the obligor. (4) So if the executor of the obligee marry the obligor, such marriage is no release of the debt, but the remedy is only suspended during the coverture. (c)

A covenant not to sue a single obligor may be pleaded as a release. (d) but as such covenant does not operate as a release in its own nature, but only by construction, to avoid circuity of action; if the obligee covenant not to sue one of two joint and several obligors, he may, notwithstanding, proceed against the other. (e) And if the covenant be not to sue for a certain time, it does not amount to a defeazance, without it forms a part of the same instrument, but is merely a covenant, for the breach of which the obligor may maintain an action. (f)

CHAPTER VI.

OF THE PROCEEDINGS AGAINST HEIR AND DEVISEE ON THE BOND OF ANCESTOR OR DEVISOR.

The heir of the obligor is chargeable when expressly named in the bond of his ancestor in respect of legal assets by descent, (a) but to render him liable it is not sufficient that he is mentioned in the condition, without being also named in the obligatory part of the bond. (4) His liability, however, does not extend beyond the value of the land; and after he has paid the debt of his ancestor to that amount, he is entitled to hold the land discharged. (c) When the obligor has heirs and lands on the part of his father, and also on the part of his mother, both heirs shall be equally charged. (d) A seisin in law is not sufficient to make the heir chargeable, but there must be a seisin in fact. (e) The possession, however, of a tenant for years is considered in law as the possession of the heir: (f) but if no person be in possession at the death of the ancestor there must be an actual entry by the heir to give him a seisin in fact. (g)

If there be a devisee as well as heir the action should be brought against either the heir or the devisee. (a) Bac. Ab. Heir; as to what are assets by descent, see 2 Saund. (c) Bushby v. Dizon, 3 B. & C., Rep. 8, d. 299. (b) Barber v. Forrester, 2 Saund., 136. (f) Id. (c) Buckley v. Nightingale, 1 Sh., 665.

<br>

### --- Page 0125 ---

Declaration.

Proceedings against heir or devisee.

Be against them jointly. If there be no heir, the action is against the devisee only. And if there be several heirs, as in the case of gavelkind or parceners, they should all be joined. (4) The heir and executor cannot be jointly sued, but separate actions may be brought against them at the same time; and if the heir be also executor, he may be sued in both capacities. (1)

The declaration should describe the defendant as heir; but it is not in general necessary to show how he became so, whether as son, grandson, &c., of the person last seised, since that matter lies more particularly within the defendant’s knowledge. If, however, the defendant is only collateral heir, the mesne descent should be stated, for if he be declared against as heir of the obligor, and it should appear in evidence on a plea of riens per descent from the obligor that the defendant is heir of the heir of the obligor, it would be a fatal variance. Thus where the obligor died seised in fee leaving issue, and the issue died without issue, whereupon the lands descended to the defendant as heir to the son of his brother, the court upon a special case gave judgment against the plaintiff, for the defendant had nothing as immediate heir to his brother. (2) So where the obligor being seised in fee died, leaving a daughter, and shortly after his death his wife gave birth to a son who lived but an hour, it was held, that this evidence did not support the issue, for the defendant had nothing from her father the obligor, but the lands came to her by descent as heir of her brother. (2) But where there has been a previous limitation of the

### --- Page 0126 ---

PROCEEDINGS AGAINST HEIR OR DEVISEE.

The estate which has become extinct, the defendant may be described as heir of the person originally seised in fee, without noticing the mesne descent. Thus, where the obligor having two sons limited an estate, of which he was seised in fee, to himself for life, remainder to his eldest son in tail, remainder to his own right heirs, and died; whereupon his eldest son became seised in tail with remainder in fee expectant, and afterwards died leaving a son who died without issue, upon whose death the premises descended to the defendant in fee, the estate tail being then extinct; it was held, that the defendant was properly described as the immediate heir of the obligor.

The heir may plead any matter which might have been set up as a defence by the ancestor; he may also deny the character in which he is sued; or admitting it, he may plead that he has nothing by descent, which plea is technically termed reins per descent, or he may plead that he has only a reversion expectant on the life of another; or has paid debts of equal or superior degree to the full extent of the lands descended; or that he retains the assets to satisfy his own debts of equal or superior degree. But in every case except where he pleads reins per descent, or denies the plaintiff’s cause of action, he should confess the action and show the certainty of assets, for by common law if issue be taken on the quantity of assets, and it be found that the heir has other lands by descent, or if the defendant plead a fact which he knows to be false, and it is found

But the heir cannot plead assets in the hands of the executors, for it is at the election of the obligor to sue either the heir or the executor. (q) Nor can he plead that he claims to retain a sum of money paid for repairs without stating them to be necessary repairs. (r)

The common replication to a plea of riens per descent is, that the defendant has assets by descent, and upon this issue, though the lands are devised to the heir, yet if the devise convey the same estate the law would have cast upon him, he takes by descent, although by the terms of the demise there is a possibility of a charge, or an actual charge or incumbrance on the lands. Person or persons, the parol shall not demur, but such action, suit, or other proceeding, shall be prosecuted and carried on in the same manner, and as effectually as any action or suit could before the passing of the act be carried on or prosecuted by or against any infant, where, according to law, the parol did not demur.”

At common law the heir might have released himself from all liability by a bona fide alienation of the lands descended before action brought. But to prevent creditors from being thus deprived of their just claims, the 3 & 4 Will. & M.c. 14, s. 5, enacted, “that where the heir should be liable to pay the debt of his ancestor in respect of lands descending to him, and should alien the same before action brought, he should be liable to an action of debt to the value of the land so aliened.” The sixth section provided, that he might “ plead to such action reiens per descent and that the plaintiff might reply, that he had lands from his ancestor before original writ brought, and if upon issue joined thereon, it should be found for the plaintiff, the jury should inquire of the value of the lands, and judgment should be given and execution awarded accordingly; but if judgment should be given against the heir by confession, without confessing assets descending, it should be for the debt and damages, without any writ to inquire of the lands,”

The same statute has also made lands in the hands of a devisee or alienee liable to the specialty creditors, who had not any remedy against the devisee at common law, and has enabled them to maintain debt upon their bonds against the devisee and heir-at-law jointly; (u) it also made the devisee in all respects chargeable,

When advisable to reply according to statute.

PROCEEDINGS AGAINST THEIR OR DEVISEE.

It will be observed, that when the defendant pleads rien per descent, the plaintiff may reply either at common law or according to the statute.(z) If he take issue according to the statute, he can have judgment and execution for debt and costs only to the value of the land.(y) And, therefore, in most cases it is advisable to reply as at common law, and in the event of a verdict against the defendant the judgment is general, in the same manner as if the action had been for his own debt.(z) In some cases, however, a special judgment may be more beneficial than a general one; for if the heir have not any other lands except those by descent, the plaintiff can only have a moiety delivered to him by an elegit on a general judgment; whereas he is entitled to the whole on a special judgment. (a) If the defendant have pleaded non est factum, or have confessed the action, and shown with certainty the assets descended, the judgment is special, that the plaintiff do recover his debt and damages to be levied of the lands descended; but if judgment be given against him on demurrer, or by default, nil dicit, or confession, without showing the assets or on any other matter whatsoever, the judgment is general. CHAPTER VII.

OF THE LIABILITY OF THE OBLIGOR GENERALLY.
AS SURETY.

Upon a breach of the condition the penalty of a bond becomes the debt in law, and is the true measure of the obligor’s liability. It has, indeed, in one or two instances, been held, that damages might be recovered beyond the amount of the penalty, (a) but it seems clear from other authorities (5) that both in law and equity the obligor is not answerable beyond the penalty of the bond and costs, (c) notwithstanding the obligee may have sustained damage exceeding that amount; and upon payment of the penalty and costs, the court will order the obligee to acknowledge satisfaction on the roll. (d) But when a judgment is recovered on the bond, and an action is brought on that judgment, interest may be given in the nature of damages, beyond the amount of the penalty of the bond. (e)

At common law the obligee might have had execution for the whole amount of the penalty, though far exceeding the sum really due, and the obligor could only obtain relief by application to a court of equity.

(a) Lonsdale v. Church, 2 T. R. 388; Francis v. Wilson, 1 R.& M. 303; Shutt v. Procter, 2 Marsh. 246, 105. (e) McClure v. Dunkin, 1 East, 1190; 6 Ves. jun. 411; Clark v. Seton, 3 Bro. Ch. Rep. 492.

(b) White v. Sealy, Doug. 49; Branguin v. Perrot, 2 W. Black. Not liable beyond the penalty and costs, though damages exceed that amount.

Where money due is less than penalty, proceedings stayed on payment of sum due and costs.

1 Saund. 580. nec

<br>

### --- Page 0131 ---

Tf the whole sum for which the bond is a security be not due, judgment is entered for the penalty.

Liability OF SURETY.

Will not extend to any subsequent change, without a clear intention appears.

To remedy this inconvenience, the 4 Ann. c. 16, s. 13, enacted, “that if at any time pending an action on a bond with a penalty, the defendant shall bring into court, where the action shall be depending, all the principal money and interest due on such bond, and also all such costs as have been expended in any suit or suits in law or equity on such bond, the money so brought in shall be deemed and taken to be in full satisfaction and discharge of the said bond, and the court shall and may give judgment to discharge every such defendant of and from the same accordingly.”

Where, however, the bond is conditioned for the payment of a sum of money at a future day, and interest in the meantime; or if it be conditioned for the payment of an annuity, or of money by instalments, and the bond becomes forfeited by non-payment of interest or of the instalments; in these and similar cases the courts will not stay proceedings before judgment, (g) but will permit it to be entered for the penalty of the bond, which will stand as a security for the growing arrears, and the execution will be restrained to the sum actually due. (g)

The liability of sureties will never be extended by implication, but the condition will in general be construed strictly in their favour. It has been established by a series of cases that where a surety is bound for the faithful services of his principal as clerk to one or more individuals, the obligation will not extend to any subsequent change, without an intention to that effect clearly appears upon the face of the bond, for it is presumed that the surety might be induced to enter into such engagement only with a view to the particular service intended. (g) Van Sandau v. Beale, 387, A. 214; Gowlett v. Hanforth, 2 W. (g) Judd v. Evans, 6 T. R. 399; Tighe v. Crafter, 2 Taunt.

<br>

### --- Page 0132 ---

LIABILITY OF SURETY.

Into such a security in consequence of the confidence which he reposed in the integrity, diligence, caution, and accuracy, of one or more persons; (4) and which reasons might not equally apply to other individuals. Thus, where the condition was that a clerk of the plaintiff should, during the time he continued in the service of the plaintiff, keep just and true accounts of all monies received and paid, and from time to time pay all monies which he should receive belonging to the plaintiff into his hands, and the obligee afterwards took a partner, it was held, that the defendant was not liable for a breach of the condition subsequently to the partnership. (4) So where the condition was that a person whom the obligee had taken as a clerk should give a true and just account, and pay to the obligee, his executors and administrators, all money which should be from time to time received for and on account of the said obligee, his executors and administrators; and the obligee having died, the plaintiff, his executor, carried on his trade and retained the clerk in his service; it was adjudged that the bond was given to the obligee as an indemnity that the clerk should be faithful to him, and should pay all monies received on his account to him or to his executors, and it was not the intention of the parties that the bond should be extended beyond the obligee’s life. (4) In like manner a condition to pay all sums advanced by three partners, does not extend to an advance made after the death of one of them and a new partner taken in. (2) And where a charter is

### --- Page 0133 ---

The principle applies equally to obligors and sureties.

Granted to a society not previously incorporated, the bond ceases to exist, notwithstanding the same persons remain governors; for the original society is thereby destroyed, and in judgment of law a new body is constituted. (m)

The doctrine established by the above cases is equally applicable to a change of the obligors; and therefore, where several partners are bound with condition to pay money advanced to meet bills drawn by them, the condition does not extend to bills drawn by the survivors after the death of one or more of them. (z) And where a surety undertakes that his principal shall account for all sums of money received by him for the obligee’s use, the guaranty does not extend to sums received by the principal after he has with the obligee’s knowledge taken a partner. (0)

Upon the same principal, if a bond be given to the sheriff by a gaoler, conditioned for the safe removal and conveyance of prisoners under the authority of writs of habeas corpus, and the sheriff on a particular occasion directs his warrant to the gaoler and a bailiff specially appointed for that time only to bring up the body of a prisoner, in the event of the prisoner escaping from the custody of the bailiff, neither principal nor sureties are liable in an action by the sheriff on the bond; for in such case, the principal would not be considered as acting under his ordinary appointment of gaoler, but under a new and different authority imposed upon him by the sheriff himself. (p)

(m) Dance v. Girdler, 1 New (0) Bellairs v. Ebsworth, 3 Camp. Rep. 34. 53.

(n) Simson v. Cooke, 8 Moore, (p) Ryland v. Lavender, 9 Moore, 588 ; S.C. 1 Bing. 452. 715 ante, p. 35.

### --- Page 0134 ---

LIABILITY OF SURETY. 111

If, therefore, it is intended that the responsibility of a surety should extend to any subsequent change, such intention should be clearly expressed upon the face of the instrument. A distinction, however, has been taken between the above cases, and where the security is given to the house or firm; in the latter instance, a change in the persons composing a partnership will not operate as a discharge of the liability of the surety.

Thus, where a bond was given to the trustees of a certain company, conditioned for the faithful performance of the duties of a clerk during his continuance in the service of the company, it was held that the bond might be put in suit notwithstanding any intermediate change of the original holders of shares by death or transfer. (q) And where the condition, after reciting that a bill in equity had been filed against two persons, was that the obligor should pay all such costs as the court should award to the defendants on the hearing of the cause, and one of the defendants died before any costs were awarded, it was held by three of the judges (Abbott, C. J., dissentiente) that that fact could not be pleaded in discharge of the bond. (7)

It has been before observed that the extent of the liability of a surety may be restrained by the recitals. This doctrine has been uniformly acted upon in favour of sureties. Thus, where it appears by the recital that the defendant is bound as surety for the due performance by his principal of the duties of any office, the liability of the surety will be limited with reference to the nature of such office. (s) If the office be an annual one, the surety will only be answerable during the period

(4) Metcalf v. Bruin, 12 East, 400. (5) The Wardens of St. Saviour’s, Southwark, v. Bostock, 2 New Rep. 261. 175.

<br>

### --- Page 0135 ---

LIABILITY OF SURETY.

Of the current year, notwithstanding his principal continues to hold office for a longer time. (c) And though it does not appear on the face of the bond to what period the office extends, the defendant may nevertheless show by pleading that its duration is limited to a particular time; (d) but if the duration of the office be prescribed by act of parliament, it will be unnecessary to plead it, for the courts will judicially notice it. (e) Thus, where the condition of the bond, in which the defendants were bound as sureties, recited that their principal was, on a particular day, appointed collector of the church-rate of a certain parish; by virtue of which office he was empowered to collect and receive all such monies as were rated and assessed on the inhabitants by virtue of the said rate, and for which he was accountable to the wardens of the grand account; and the defendants were bound for duly accounting by their principal of all monies collected and received by him on account of the above rate, and also on all and every other rate or rates thereafter to be made or collected by him. The defendants pleaded performance by the principal in the terms of the condition; to which plea the plaintiffs replied, that the principal was on a certain day appointed collector of the church-rate of the parish for one whole year then next ensuing, and at the expiration of that year was again appointed, and so on from year to year for a certain space of time: it was held upon demurrer, that as the replication admitted the office to be an annual one, the defendants were only liable during that period. (f)

Peppin v. Cooper, 2 B.& A. 37; Peppin v. Cooper, 2 B. & A. 431. 437.

Hassell v. Long, 2 M. & S. 364; Curling v. Chalklen, 3 M.&S. 502. 175,

Leadley v. Evans, 2 Bingh.

### --- Page 0136 ---

LIABILITY OF SURETY,

The principle here laid down is not confined to cases of a surety to the person holding the office, but extends also to his deputies: thus, where the condition of the bond was, that a collector of the poor-rate should from time to time and at all times when thereunto required, produce to the churchwardens and overseers, or to their successors, all such sums of money as should come to his hands pursuant to and in the execution of his office ; it was held, that as the offices of overseer and churchwardens were annual offices, the office of collector, who was in the nature of their deputy, must be confined to the same period; and therefore the obligor was not answerable for monies received by him on account of any year subsequently to that in which the obligees were in office. (2) It does not however necessarily follow that the deputy of an annual officer must be an annual officer himself. In the last-mentioned case there was nothing to show any intention that the deputy should continue in office a longer period than his principal; but where the condition of a bond recited a statute, and that in pursuance thereof the principal obligor had been appointed by the churchwardens and parishioners of a certain parish collector of the poor-rate to be levied and raised in the parish: and the condition was, that the collector should account for all monies so collected and received by him by virtue of the statute, and it appeared that there was nothing on the face of the statute to limit the period of office to a year, the bond was considered to extend to the whole period of time during which the principal obligor continued in the office of collector. (a)

(2) Leadley v. Evans, 2 Bingh. (a) Curling v. Chalklen, 3 M. & S. C. 9 Moore, 102. S. 502.

I

113

Principle extends to deputies of persons holding office.

<br>

### --- Page 0137 ---

Sureties cannot plead the laches of their principal in discharge of their obligation.

Liable to one another for contribution,

LIABILITY OF SURETY.

A bond being an instrument under seal, the obligation created by it can only be discharged by an instrument of an equally high nature; (b) and therefore the laches of the obligee in not calling upon the principal for payment so soon as he might have done is not an estoppel at law in an action against the sureties; (c) neither can the giving time to the principal by parol agreement be pleaded in discharge of their liability, (d) though that circumstance might induce a court of equity to interfere, or would be a ground for an application to the equitable jurisdiction of a court of law, (e) which will always be exercised where the surety is placed in a worse situation.

In the event of any one surety being compelled to pay the whole sum recovered, he may maintain an action against his co-surety for contribution, (f) even though he had given a subsequent security to the obligees, under which he paid the sum named in the bond without the knowledge or consent of such co-surety. (g) And if several persons are bound as sureties in separate bonds, and any one of them be compelled to pay the whole debt, the others are liable for contribution in proportion to the penalties of their respective bonds. (h)

CHAPTER VIII,

OF CHANGES BY DEATH, MARRIAGE, BANKRUPTCY, or INSOLVENCY.

The personal claim founded upon an obligation survives the death of the obligor, and therefore his executors are bound though not named in the bond. So upon the death of the obligee, his interest vests in his personal representatives, though not included in the terms of it; for the executors and administrators of every person are implied in himself.

A bond given to a single woman does not upon marriage vest absolutely in the husband, but being a mere chose in action, must first be reduced into possession, and therefore he cannot sue alone upon it, but his wife must be joined. If the husband neglects during his lifetime to reduce it into possession, his wife, and not his personal representatives, will be entitled to it.

If the bond be given to husband and wife, both may join in the action, in which case, if the husband die after judgment, it will survive to the wife. Or the husband may disagree to his wife's right to the bond, and sue alone upon it; but if he die without having put

BANKRUPTCY.

Judges were of opinion that the debt was only suspended, as it was a contingency that could not by any possibility happen during the marriage; but Holt, C.J. considered that a bond debt was a present debt, and the condition was not precedent, but subsequent, which made it a present duty, and consequently it was released by the marriage. In a later case, however, this decision was fully examined, and the Court having expressed great dissatisfaction at the doctrine laid down by Holt, C.J., decided that a bond given by the obligor to the obligee before marriage, conditioned for the payment of money after the obligor’s death, and intended for the obligee’s benefit if she should survive him, is not released by the marriage; and that if the marriage be pleaded in bar to an action on the bond, the plaintiff may reply, stating the purposes for which the bond was given. (r)

It has been before observed that upon a breach of the condition, the penalty of the bond becomes a debt in law. If therefore the bond be forfeited at the time of the bankruptcy of the obligor, the sum claimed must be proved under the commission, or the certificate will be a bar to any future proceedings. And the law is the same whether the bond be for the payment of money or for the performance or forbearance of any act. (s) But where there is not an existing debt, or if there be continuing damage, which cannot be ascertained at the time of the bankruptcy, the certificate will be no bar to an action on the bond. Inolvency

BANKRUPTCY.

Bankrupt Act (u) has however enabled a creditor to apply to the commissioners to set a value on any debt payable on a contingency, and to admit proof of the amount ascertained. And annuity (v), bottomry (w), and respondentia bonds are expressly made provable under the commission, and therefore in these cases the certificate would be a bar.

Sureties and bail for the bankrupt are also enabled to prove their demand, if they have actually paid the debt of the principal. But this provision does not extend to the bankruptcy of a co-surety, and, therefore, where the principal and four sureties were jointly and severally bound for the payment of money and interest, and one of the sureties became bankrupt and obtained his certificate, and at the time of his bankruptcy the bond was forfeited, it was held, that as the bond could not have been proved under the commission, the certificate was no bar to an action for contribution against the surety who had been bankrupt.

The Insolvent Act (x) directs a prisoner complying with the provisions of that act to be discharged as to the several debts and sums of money due or claimed to be due at the time of filing his petition to the several persons named in his schedule as creditors. (y) What is therefore above stated with respect to the bond being an actual debt, applies as well to insolvency as to bankruptcy. (z) Where a bond to replace stock at a certain day, and in the meantime pay the dividends, became forfeited by nonpayment of the dividends, but

CHAPTER IX.

OF ALTERATION AND CANCELLATION OF A BOND.

If a bond, after its execution, be altered by the obligee in a material part, either by rasure, interliniation, addition, or drawing a line through the words, whether the original writing be legible or not, it will be void at common law. (a)

But an alteration by the obligor will not destroy the bond, for a party cannot take advantage of his own wrong. ()

So it would seem in the present day an alteration by a stranger in a material part would not avoid the bond, provided the contents of it could be proved. (c) If the alteration be made in an immaterial part, the obligation will not be affected by it. (d)

Where the bond was conditioned for payment of one hundred pounds by six equal payments of £16: 13s. 4d., on a certain day in every year, until the full sum of one pound was paid; and a person, in whose custody the bond was kept, without the knowledge of either party, inserted the word hundred between one and pound; the alteration was held immaterial. (e)

(a) Pigot’s case, 11 Co. 27. 207.

(b) Shep. Touch. 69.

(c) Henfree v. Bromley, 5 East, 809; Waugh v. Bussell, 5 Taunt. 707; S.C. 14 Marsh, 214.

(d) Shep. Touch. 69.

<br>

### --- Page 0144 ---

OF ALTERATION OF BOND.

So if a blank space be left for a Christian name or addition, which is filled up after the execution of the bond, with the assent of the parties, the alteration will not affect the validity of the bond. (f) And where two persons executed a bond, and afterwards the name and addition of another was interlined, and he also sealed and delivered the bond, with the consent of all parties, it was held to be the bond of all three. (g) Also where a party executed a bond, with blank spaces for the name and sum, and sent an agent to raise money on it, and the obligee having lent half the sum required, the agent accordingly filled up the blanks with the sum and the obligee’s name, and delivered the bond to him; on plea of non est factum, the bond was considered well executed. (4) But where, after the execution of a bond by two only of three joint and several obligors, the name of another obligor is substituted for the party who had not executed, without the knowledge of the obligors, it would seem the bond is void as against them, notwithstanding they have afterwards assented to the alteration and paid some instalments due on the bond. (é)

If a material alteration be made after the execution is perfected, though with the consent of all parties, a new stamp will be requisite. “Cancellation,

Delivery for the purpose of cancellation,

OF ALTERATION OF BOND.

If the seal be broken off by accident, or by a stranger, or by the obligor, the bond will remain in force, on proof that it was sealed and delivered, and thus accidentally or wrongfully cancelled; but if the obligee tear off the seal with an intention to cancel the bond, the obligation will be destroyed; for though no deed, which has once passed an estate or interest, can be avoided by cancellation, yet it is otherwise with a bond, which is merely obligatory upon the person. (m) And if the bond be joint, or joint and several, the tearing off the seal of one of the obligors will avoid it as to the rest; though if the bond be several only, the others are not thereby discharged. (n)

If an obligee deliver up a bond to the obligor to be cancelled, which the latter omits to do, and the bond again gets into the hands of the obligee, the obligation still remains in force at law, and the obligor can only obtain relief in equity. (o)

Dest is the proper form of action upon bonds, whether single or with condition. (a) Covenant will also lie, though that remedy is seldom used in practice. (4)
In general debt cannot be maintained for the recovery of money payable by instalments, unless the whole amount be due; (c) but in the case of a bond, with condition to pay money by instalments, the bond is forfeited by the nonpayment of one instalment, and the penalty becomes a debt in law, for which an action of debt may be maintained. (d) And it may be observed that the execution of a bond creates a debt or duty, which adheres to the obligor wherever contracted, and therefore a bond executed abroad may be put in suit in this country, but in that case the declaration should state the true place at which it was made. (e) Where the obligation is to pay a sum of money at several days, the obligee cannot maintain an action of debt until the last day be past. (f)

In declaring on a bond it is not necessary to show a consideration, (r) but the declaration proceeds at once, without any inducement, to state that the defendant on a certain day by his writing obligatory, sealed with his seal, and shown to the Court, acknowledged himself held and firmly bound to the plaintiff in a sum of money to be paid to the plaintiff, and it then alleges a breach in the nonpayment of that sum.

There is some doubt by what name the obligor should be sued, or the obligee sue, where they are described by one name in the bond, and have executed by another. It has been decided that a declaration against an obligor by his right name, stating that he executed the bond by a wrong name, is bad. (s) But it seems the proper course is to declare by or against a party by the name in the bond, unless he is there called by different names, in which case he should sue or be sued by the name by which he executed. Time.
