OF THE REMEDY BY ACTION.

With respect to the statement of the day on which the bond was made, it should be observed, that every deed is supposed to be delivered and made on the day on which it bears date; but when the bond has been delivered after the time of its date, and that fact is material to the obligee’s right of action, as in the case of a release having been given by him to the obligor after the date, but before the delivery of the bond, the declaration should state the bond as bearing date on the day therein mentioned, but delivered afterwards; (x) for if the obligee declare on the bond as made on a particular day, he cannot afterwards reply that it was delivered on another and a different day, since, as a bond takes effect from the day of its delivery and not of its date, the replication would be inconsistent with, and a departure from, the declaration. (y)

The declaration should show that the bond was sealed by the defendant; but if the words “sealed with his seal” be omitted, the defect will be cured by verdict or pleading over; for when it is stated that the defendant “by his writing obligatory acknowledged himself bound, all necessary circumstances will be intended.” (z)

In the case of bonds executed abroad, which do not by the custom of the country require a seal, (a) the declaration should not describe them as sealed instruments, but as writings obligatory subscribed by the obligor, and duly made according to the custom of the country. (b)

Where the penalty of a bond stood as a security for the payment of a less sum, or for the performance of several distinct acts, the obligee was entitled, upon a breach of the condition, to recover at law the full amount of the penalty, without any regard to the damage he had actually sustained. In such cases the obligor’s only remedy was by application to a court of equity for relief upon payment of the sum really due. For the purpose of rendering this circuitous redress unnecessary, the 8th and 9th Will. 3, c. 11, sect. 8, enacted, “that in all actions upon any bond or bonds, or on any penal sum for non-performance of any covenants or agreements in any indenture, deed, or writing contained, the plaintiff or plaintiffs may assign as many breaches as he or they shall think fit, and the jury, upon trial of such action or actions, shall and may assess not only such damages and costs of suit as have heretofore been usually done in such cases, but also damages for such of the said breaches so to be assigned as the plaintiff upon the trial of the issues shall prove.

(m) Sec 3 & 4 Will. 4, c. 42, 8. 23.

Assignment
of Breaches.

<br>

### --- Page 0153 ---

OF ASSIGNMENT OF BREACHES, to have been broken; and that the like judgment shall be entered on such verdict as heretofore hath been usually done in such like actions; and if judgment shall be given for the plaintiff on a demurrer, or by confession of nihil dicit, the plaintiff upon the roll may suggest as many breaches of the covenants and agreements as he shall think fit, upon which shall issue a writ to the sheriff of the county where the action shall be brought, to summon a jury to appear before the justices or justice of assize or nisi prius of that county, to inquire of the truth of every one of those breaches, and to assess the damages that the plaintiff shall have sustained thereby, and in case the defendant or defendants, after such judgment entered and before any execution executed, shall pay unto the court where the action shall be brought, to the use of the plaintiff or plaintiffs, or his or their executors or administrators, such damages so to be assessed by reason of all or any of the breaches of such covenants, together with the costs of suit, a stay of execution of the said judgment shalt be entered upon record; or, if by reason of any execution executed the plaintiff or plaintiffs, or his or their executors or administrators, shall be fully paid or satisfied all such damages to be assessed, together with his or their costs of suit, and all reasonable charges and expenses for executing the said execution, the body, lands, or goods of the defendant, shall be thereupon forthwith discharged from the said execution, which shall likewise be entered upon record;

(n) As to entering up judgment of the county where the action is on default, &c. see 3 Wms. Saund. 187, n.

(0) The 3 & 4 W. 4, c.42, 861, h' altered this enactment, and requires the writ to direct the sheriff

<br>

### --- Page 0154 ---

OF ASSIGNMENT OF BREACHES.

But, notwithstanding, in each case such judgment shall remain, continue, and be as a further security to answer to the plaintiff or plaintiffs, and his or their executors or administrators, such damages as shall or may be sustained for further breach of any covenant or covenants, in the same indenture, deed, or writing contained.”

Though the word “may” is used in the above statute, it has been decided that it is compulsory on the plaintiff to assign breaches in all cases which fall within it. (p) Questions have, however, frequently arisen as to the description of bonds within the terms of the statute. The chief object of the legislature having been to enable a jury to ascertain the damage sustained by a breach of the condition, without compelling the obligor to resort to a Court of Equity; it would seem that it may in general be determined in what cases it is necessary to assign breaches by considering whether the precise sum due can be ascertained without the intervention of a jury or a Court of Equity. (p) Thus bonds for the payment of annuities or of money by instalments, (r) for the performance of an award, (s) or for the performance of covenants and agreements, are within the provisions of the statute. So where a bond appeared conditioned for the payment of a sum certain, but by an indenture of even date it was agreed that the plaintiffs should be at liberty to enter up judgment when they thought fit; it was held to be substantially a bond conditioned for the performance of an agreement, and that breaches ought to be assigned. (r) Murray v. Stair, 2 B. & C. 558; Willoughby v. Swinton, 6 East, 90; (q) Walcot v. Goulding, 8 T.R. 550; 3 Moore & S. 538; 126; Collins v. Collins, 2 Burr. 820. (s) Welch v. Ireland, 6 East, 613.

The statute is compulsory.

Bonds within the terms of the statute include those for the payment of annuities or of money by instalments, for the performance of an award, or for the performance of covenants and agreements.

<br>

### --- Page 0155 ---

Bonds not within terms of statute, OF ASSIGNMENT OF BREACHES.

assigned. (f) Also where the obligee of an indemnity bond is sued for damages in respect of the matter of the indemnity, which are recovered against him, and he afterwards proceeds against the obligor on his indemnity, the obligee should assign as a breach not only the damages and costs recovered against him, but also his own costs sustained in defending the suit, although he may in fact as yet have paid no part of such costs. (2)

But bonds for the payment of a sum certain at a day certain, and post obit bonds, (x) are not within the statute; neither are bail bonds, (y) nor replevin bonds; (z) for in the two latter cases the courts of law can exercise an equitable jurisdiction; nor bonds given by the petitioning creditor of a bankrupt; (a) and if an annuity be secured by a warrant of attorney to confess judgment on a mutuus breaches need not be assigned. (b)

Also bonds which fall within the provisions of the 4 Anne, c. 16, s. 13, which empowers the Court, pending an action on the bond, to discharge the defendant upon payment of the principal and interest due and costs. So where a bond is conditioned for the payment of a sum of money on a day certain, with interest in the mean time, pursuant to the provisions and stipulations contained in an indenture, upon the non-performance of which both principal and interest became due; it is not necessary to assign breaches according

OF ASSIGNMENT OF BREACHES.

It was formerly considered that breaches should be assigned in the declaration, because if the defendant pleaded any plea upon which the plaintiff might at common law have taken issue, without assigning a breach, it would be incorrect to assign them in the replication. It is now, however, established that the plaintiffs not only may, but that it is advisable (unless judgment by default be expected) to reserve them for the replication, since the defendant in rejoining can only present one answer to each breach, whereas in pleading to the declaration he may with leave of the Court answer each breach by any number of pleas.

If the defendant suffers judgment by default, or the plaintiff gets judgment upon demurrer, or where the plea cannot lead to an issue on the breaches, as upon non est factum, or that the bond was obtained by fraud and covin, the plaintiff may suggest breaches on the roll. But if the plea be such that breaches may be assigned upon it, as a plea of performance, and the plaintiff takes issue on the plea and enters a suggestion of breaches on the record, no damages can be assessed on them, and the Court will award a repleader.

(c) James v. Thomas, 5 B. & (g) 1 Chit. Plead. 506. Adol. 40; S.C. 2 N. & M. 663; (h) 3 Wms. Saund. 187 a, n.; 3 Moore & S., 158.

Ethersey v. Jackson, 8 T.R. 255;

(d) Rex v. Peto, 1 Y.& J. 171. Home ray v. Righy, 5 M. & S. 60.

(e) 2 Saund. 187 a, n. (i) Plomer v. Ross, 5 Taunt. 86.

Whether to be assigned in declaration or replication,

Breach how assigned.

OF ASSIGNMENT OF BREACHES.

Before the above statute the plaintiff could assign only one breach of the condition, or the declaration would have been bad for duplicity. It is not, however, necessary in a declaration, assigning more than one breach, to refer to its being according to the statute; nor is it necessary in a replication, assigning only one breach, but if the replication assigns more than one it is otherwise. (t) The breach should be assigned with certainty and particularity, though the superaddition of immaterial allegations will not vitiate it, if it be in other respects good. (f) To a plea of non damnum, the replication should show how the plaintiff was damnified. (m) To a plea of no award the replication must set out the whole award and also assign a breach; (n) and to a plea of performance to a bond conditioned to render an account of monies received, it is necessary to show in the replication that monies were received to be accounted for. (o) Though where a fact lies more within the knowledge of the defendant than the plaintiff, a general allegation is sufficient. Thus where the condition was to account for and pay over to the plaintiff, as treasurer of a charity, such voluntary contributions as should be collected for the use of the charity; and to a plea of performance the plaintiffs replied that divers sums of money had been received, amounting to the sum of £100, from divers persons for divers voluntary contributions, for the use of the said charity, which had not been accounted for or paid.

(k) Tombs v. Painter, 13 East, 1. (n) 2 Wms. Saund, 62, ch. 55
(l) Stothert v. Goodfellow, 1 N. R. 1 id. 103, n. 1.
(m) M. 262. (o) Serva v. Wright, 6 Taunt. 45 ;

### --- Page 0158 ---

OF ASSIGNMENT OF BREACHES.

Over, the replication on demurrer was considered sufficiently certain. Where the condition was not to assault, molest, or injure the plaintiff, a replication alleging that the defendant assaulted, &c., by the beating and ill treating the plaintiff, was considered a sufficient assignment of the breach of the condition, although not in formal terms, according to the statute. And where to debt on bond, conditioned for payment of a sum of money on a day specified, according to the tenor and proviso contained in a certain indenture, and for the performance of the covenants therein, the defendant pleaded that there were no negative or disjunctive covenants in the indenture, and that he paid the money on the day specified, and performed all the covenants and provisoes in the indenture; and the defendant took issue generally on the nonpayment of the money, and concluded to the country. Such replication was held proper, as the only point in issue was the payment of money. So where to a declaration on bond, conditioned amongst other things for the payment of £3000, it is pleaded that all sums of money which became due on the bond were paid; the replication may deny the words of the plea, without assigning any particular breach.

### --- Page 0159 ---

Oyer.

OF ASSIGNMENT OF BREACHES.

Hond, non est factum is the proper plea.(r) In reference to this plea it was formerly material to observe the distinction between a bond absolutely void or merely voidable. In the former case the plea of non est jactum was sufficient;(s) but in the latter instance it was necessary to plead the special matter. Now, however, the plea of non est factum only operates as a denial of the execution of the deed in point of fact; and all other defences must be specially pleaded, including matters which make the bond absolutely void, as well as those which render it voidable. (t)

In order to take advantage of a defect not apparent on the face of the declaration the defendant must crave oyer of the bond and condition, or either of them. (u)

So if he would plead payment or performance of the condition not set out in the declaration, he should pray oyer of it.(v) But if oyer of the bond be craved the defendant is not entitled to oyer of the condition, without that be craved also, for the obligation and condition are separate and distinct instruments. (w) After craving oyer the whole condition should be set forth, with all the recitals, for if the defendant omit any part, or do not state it correctly, the plaintiff may pray to have the bond or condition or either of them enrolled, and then demur, (x) or sign judgment as for want of a plea. In practice it is not usual to set forth the bond without the terms of it are material to the defence ; and unless the omission be material, the plaintiff may be compelled to state the whole bond. (y)

(r) A specialty being the foundation of the action, nil debet was always bad on demurrer, but now, by R. H. T. 4 W. 4, the plea of nil debet shall not be allowed in any action. And see 11 East, 1. R. 371; Com. Dig. pl. 1.

OF THE PLEADINGS.

Some use is intended to be made of it, it is sufficient to pray oyer of the condition only. (y)

The defendant by craving oyer may in some cases aid a defect in the declaration, as where the plaintiff declared upon a certain writing, and the defendant, by praying oyer of the condition of the said writing obligatory, admitted it to be a bond. (z)

The requisites of a good bond and condition, and the performance thereof, and also the effect of alteration having been fully considered, the matters of defence relating thereto may be easily collected. And it may be here observed that the obligor is not restrained from pleading any matter which shows the bond to be void, though such plea be inconsistent with the language of the bond. (a) The pleas however of performance, accord and satisfaction, duress, set-off, and the limitation of action, require some particular notice.

Where the bond is conditioned for the performance of covenants which are in the affirmative, a plea of general performance is proper, and the plaintiff must reply, showing a particular breach. (5) But if there be any specific or particular thing to be performed, or

Accord and satisfaction,

OF THE PLEADINGS.

The covenants are in the negative or disjunctive, performance of each must be particularly stated. (c)

To a single bill or bond, accord and satisfaction can by deed only be pleaded; for an obligation under seal cannot be discharged but by an instrument of as high a nature as the obligation itself. (d) So also to bond with condition, satisfaction after breach of the condition should be by deed, for by non-performance of the condition the bond becomes single and the penalty is the debt in law. (e) As far as can be collected from the old authorities, an opinion seems to have prevailed that accord and satisfaction before a breach of the condition was a good plea if pleaded to the condition and not to the obligation. (f) This point however does not appear to have been anywhere expressly decided. (g)

Where satisfaction is by deed its value is immaterial; but assuming that it may be pleaded to the condition without deed, it must be of something valuable in contemplation of law; and therefore the release of an equity of redemption, (4) or one bond in lieu of another, (é) is no satisfaction. So payment of a part before the time, and a promise to pay the residue at a future day, cannot be pleaded; (4) yet it seems that payment of a less sum before the day, and acceptance thereof in full satisfaction, is a good plea. (2)

(c) See 1 Wms. Saund. 117, n. 1, and 3 Chit. 985.

OF THE PLEADINGS.

Duress of imprisonment is a good plea to debt on bond, but the duress must be of the person of the defendant or his wife; and therefore a surety in a joint and several bond cannot plead the duress of his principal to an action against himself. (m) In general it must appear that the imprisonment was unlawful; (n) but if illegal force be used, or the party be made to endure unnecessary privation or hardship, it will constitute duress notwithstanding the imprisonment was by virtue of legal process. (o)

The 2 G. 2, c. 22, enabled a defendant, in the case of mutual debts, to set off one debt against the other. It was doubtful, however, upon the construction of this statute, whether a debt on simple contract could be set off against a bond, being a specialty, and also whether the penalty of the bond was to be considered the debt. To remove these difficulties the 8 G. 2, c. 24, 8. 5, (which made the above act perpetual,) enacts and declares “that mutual debts may be set off against each other notwithstanding such debts were deemed in law to be of a different nature, unless in cases where either of the said debts should accrue by reason of a penalty contained in any bond or specialty; and in all cases where either the debt for which the action is brought or the debt intended to be set against the same hath accrued by reason of any such penalty, the debt intended to be set-off shall be pleaded in bar, in which plea shall be shown how much is due on either side; and in case the plaintiff shall recover in any such action a judgment shall be entered for no more than shall

(nn) Huscombe v. Standing, Cro. 1 Lev. 89. Jas, 187. (o) 2 Inst. 482; Sel. N. P. 548. (n) Com. Dig. Plead. 2H. 19;

Set-off.

139

<br>

### --- Page 0163 ---

Debts must be due in the same right.

OF THE PLEADINGS.

It would be deviating from the plan of the present treatise to enter fully into the law of set-off; but the general rules on that subject will be illustrated so far as they are applicable to bonds.

The debts sued for and intended to be set off must be due in the same right; and therefore a debt due to the obligor in right of his wife cannot be set off in an action against him on his own bond. (p) And where an officer of the Palace Court sued on a bail-bond conditioned for the appearance of A. B. to answer C. D. in a plea of trespass on the case, it was held that the defendant could not plead by way of set-off a greater sum due to him from the plaintiff by a simple contract, for the plaintiff sued in the nature of a trustee and not in his own right; though if the bond had been given to the sheriff and assigned according to the statute, it would have been otherwise. (g) But it is a good plea in an action by a trustee to show that money is owing from the cestui que use to the defendant. (r) A joint debt cannot be set off against a separate demand, or a separate demand against a joint one. (s) But a debt on a joint and several bond may be set off to an action brought by one of the obligors; (¢) and if such bond be executed by one of the obligors only it may be set off against a claim by him.

(p) Bul. N. P. 179. (g) Grant v. The Royal Exchange (4) Hutchinson v. Sturges, Willes, Assurance Company, Tidd’s Prac. 265; Kinnerley v. Hossack, 2 Taunt. (r) Bottomley v. Brooke, cited in 170. (t) Winch v. Keeley, 1 T. R. 621; (t) Fletcher v. Dyche, 2 T. R. 32. (g) Gleadow v. Atkin, 554 C. & J.

<br>

### --- Page 0164 ---

OF THE PLEADINGS.

The debt claimed and the sum to be set off must be an actual money demand; and therefore there cannot be a set-off where the action is brought for uncertain damages, as to a bond for the performance of covenants. (u) Nor can an unliquidated demand, or uncertain damages, though secured by a penalty, be made the subject of a set-off. But where a bond is conditioned for the due performance of certain work in a limited time, or payment of a stipulated sum weekly, such weekly payments are in the nature of liquidated damages, and not by way of penalty, and may be set off in an action brought by the obligor against the obligee. (y) Also a bond for the payment of an annuity is within the statute of set-off. (=)

The sum intended to be set off must be a legal and not a mere equitable claim, and therefore a defendant cannot plead by way of set-off a bond debt of the plaintiff assigned to the defendant by another, to whom, and for whose use, it was originally given. (a)

It must be a subsisting legal demand, and therefore a debt barred by the statute of limitations or satisfied by the discharge of the debtor out of execution, cannot be set off. (c)

The sum to be set off must be actually due and payable at the commencement of the action, and therefore

(y) Fletcher v. Dyche, 2 T. R. 32.

Law of set-off extended, Replication to set-off, Limitation of action, Infants, married women, &c.

OF THE PLEADINGS.

Money secured by bond, not due at the time of the writ issued, cannot be set off. (d)

The Bankrupt Act, 6 G. 4, c. 16, s. 50, has extended the law of set-off to mutual credit as well as mutual debt. (e)

The plaintiff in his replication may traverse either the amount alleged to be due from him, or the defendant’s set-off. (f) If he should reply that more was due on the bond than the sum named in the plea, and fail in proving that allegation, he would be nonsuited. (g) The plaintiff may also reply non est factum, or admitting the bond, show any matter in discharge or avoidance of it.

Formerly there was no statutory enactment to limit the time for bringing an action on bond; but after a period of twenty years, without demand made, it was presumed to be satisfied. (h) But the 3 & 4 W. 4, c. 42, s. 3, requires all actions of debt on bond to be commenced and sued within ten years after the sessions in which that act passed, or within twenty years after the cause of action or suit.

The 4th section enacts “that if any person entitled to such action or suit shall be, at the time the cause of action accrued, within the age of twenty-one years, feme covert, non compos mentis, or beyond seas, then such person shall be at liberty to bring the same action, so as they commence the same within such times after their being of full age, discovert, of sound memory, or returned from beyond the seas, as other persons.” (d) Evans v. Prosser, 3 T. R. (f) Symmons v. Knox, 3 T. R. 186; Rogerson v. Ladbrooke, 165. Bing. 93. (g) Bell v. Shaw, Holt’s N. P. (e) See Ross v. Sims, 1 B. & Adol. 521. (h) Oswald v. Legh, 1 T. R. 270.

APPENDIX.

If the said bailiff shall safely conduct all persons in his custody as bailiff aforesaid to the common gaol by law appointed for keeping the prisoners of the said sheriff, for debt or on any civil process, immediately after the expiration of twenty-four hours from the time of arrest or detention, if in custody only on mesne process; and immediately after the arrest or detention of any prisoner in execution, unless discharged out of custody by due course of law; AND also, if the said bailiff shall make a true return and inventories of all goods and chattels seized in execution by him as bailiff to the said sheriff, and before removal thereof pay the rent in arrear not exceeding one year, pursuant to the statute in that case made and provided, and all taxes due in respect thereof, pursuant to the statute, and shall indemnify the said sheriff on account of any mistake or default relating thereto: AND also, if the said bailiff shall pay to the said sheriff, under-sheriff, or deputies the consideration or purchase-money mentioned in every bill of sale, or assignment, executed by the said sheriff, under-sheriff, or deputies, at the request of the said bailiff, notwithstanding the acknowledgment of the receipt thereof by the said sheriff contained in any such bill of sale or assignment; AND also, if the said bailiff shall and will forthwith pay to the said sheriff, under-sheriff, or deputies, all monies received by the said bailiff on any arrest or levy by him made, or with which he shall be entrusted for the said sheriff, without deduction; AND also, if the said bailiff shall certify and return to the said sheriff, under-sheriff, or deputies, at the office of the said sheriff, all unexecuted warrants of the said sheriff, on or before the twentieth day of September next, and make the like additional return up to the twenty-seventh day of the said last-mentioned month; AND also, if the said bailiff shall, pursuant to an act passed in the thirty-second year of the reign of King George the Second, intituled, “An act for relief of debtors with respect to the imprisonment of their persons, and to oblige debtors who shall continue in execution in prison beyond a certain time, and for sums not exceeding what are mentioned in the act, to make discovery of and deliver upon oath their estates for their creditors’ benefit,” show and deliver to all such prisoners in his custody as bailiff copies of the clauses therein directed to be shown.

### --- Page 0186 ---

APPENDIX.

such prisoners; AND ALSO, if the said bailiff shall in all things truly, lawfully, and honestly demean and behave himself as bailiff aforesaid, and faithfully and diligently serve and attend the said sheriff, his under-sheriff, and their deputies, and in due and lawful manner all their and every of their lawful commands or directions, touching any manner of service, incident or belonging to the said office of sheriff, shall and will execute and perform; AND ALSO, if any writ of attachment shall issue, or any rule or order of court for such writ shall be made, or any verdict of judgment given against the said sheriff or under-sheriff, or either of them, their or either of their executors or administrators, or against the under-sheriff, in any case wherein the said bailiff shall have made any arrest or levy, in the name of or under the authority of the said sheriff, the said bailiff shall and will immediately pay or cause to be paid to the said sheriff, or undersheriff, or deputies, or one of them, the debt, damages, costs, and charges, in every such case paid by them, or any of them, or for payment whereof the said sheriff or under-sheriff may be liable; AND ALSO, if the said bailiff shall well and truly pay or cause to be paid to the said sheriff, his under-sheriff, or deputies, or one of them, the costs and charges of prosecuting or opposing any motion in or application to any court touching or concerning any matter wherein the said bailiff shall act or assume to act as bailiff to the said sheriff, and the costs and charges which the said sheriff may be called upon to pay to any party or parties, person or persons, by reason of any such prosecution or opposition; In the Court of King’s Bench [C. P. or Exch. of Pleas.]
The day of ——, in the year of our Lord 183—.

[Commencement as above, p. 169.]—For rHat wueEReas the defendant heretofore, to wit, on the day of ——, in the year of our Lord » by his certain writing obligatory, sealed with his seal, and now shown to the court of our lord the king before the king himself (6) here, the date whereof is a certain day and year therein in that behalf mentioned, to wit, the day and year aforesaid, acknowledged himself to be held and firmly bound to the plaintiff in the sum of pounds above demanded to be paid to the plaintiff when he the defendant should be thereunto afterwards requested ;* yet the defendant (although often requested so to do) hath not as yet paid to the plaintiff the said sum of pounds, or any part thereof, To the plaintiff's damage of ——(c) pounds ; and thereupon he brings his suit, &c.

(a) The penalty of the bond.

(b) If in the Exchequer, say, “and now shown to the barons of his majesty’s Exchequer here.” In the Common Pleas, the profert is usually at the end of declaration, as follows: ‘ And the plaintiff brings here into court the said writing obligatory, sealed as aforesaid, which gives sufficient evidence to the said court here of the debt aforesaid, in form aforesaid, the date whereof is the day and year in that behalf above-mentioned.”

(c) In debt, the damages are merely nominal, and a small sum, as five or ten pounds, is usually inserted.

PLEAS.

AND the defendant, by E. F. his attorney, (6) (or in person,) craves oyer of the said supposed writing obligatory in the said declaration mentioned, and it is read to him (c) (&c.) ; he also craves oyer of the condition (c) of the said supposed writing obligatory, and it is read to him in these words,
“Whereas &c. [here set forth the condition, with the recitals verbatim,] which being read and heard, the defendant saith, that the said supposed writing obligatory is not his deed, and of this he puts himself upon the country, &c.

And for a further plea in this behalf, the defendant (e) saith, that he the defendant, on the said day of [insert date], in the year of our Lord [insert year], in the said condition of the said writing obligatory mentioned, paid to the plaintiff the said sum of [insert amount] pounds in the said condition mentioned, together with all interest then due thereon, according to the

(a) This form is proper when the defendant pleads double, but otherwise it is usual to plead non est factum, without setting out the condition upon oyer.

(b) The formal defence hitherto made use of is no longer required. R. H. T. 4 W. 4, c. 210,

(c) Ante, p. 136.

(d) Where the defendant pleaded double, the plea formerly commenced with an allegation that the defendant, “by leave of the court for that purpose first had and obtained, according to the form of the statute in such case made and provided, saith, that the plaintiff ought not to have or maintain his said action thereof against him.” But now it is not necessary to refer to the leave of the court, R. H. T. 4 W. 4, 211, and in a plea or subsequent pleading, intended to be pleaded in bar of the whole action generally, it is unnecessary to use any allegation of actionem non, or to the like effect, R. H. T. 4 W. 4, c. 9.

(e) Ante, p. 38.

<br>

APPENDIX.

Bond accompanied with a deposit of title deeds, for making a mortgage, wadset, or other security on any estate, or property therein comprised. See Mortgage, p. 207.

Bond in England and personal bond in Scotland, of any kind whatever, not otherwise charged in this schedule, nor expressly exempted from stamp duty ............ 115 0

Heritable Bond in Scotland of any kind whatever, not otherwise charged in this schedule, nor expressly exempted from all stamp duty.. 115 0

General Directions RESPECTING Bonds.

Where any such bond as aforesaid, together with any
schedule, receipt, or other matter, put or indorsed thereon,
or annexed thereto, shall contain 2,160 words or upwards,
then shall be charged for every entire quantity of 1,080 words
contained therein, over and above the first 1,080 words, a
further progressive duty of 11. 5s.

And where any such bond as aforesaid shall be given as a
security for the payment of a sum of money, and also of a
share in any of the stocks or funds before-mentioned, or an
annuity, or both, or for the payment of an annuity, and also
of a share in the said stocks or funds, the proper ad valorem
duty shall be charged in respect of each.

<br>

### --- Page 0228 ---

APPENDIX.

And where any such bond as aforesaid shall be given as a security for the payment or transfer to different persons, of separate and distinct sums of money, or annuities, or shares in any of the stocks or funds before-mentioned, the proper ad valorem duty shall be charged in respect of each separate and distinct sum of money, or annuity, or share in any of the said stocks or funds therein specified and secured, and not upon the aggregate amount thereof.

And where any bond in England shall be given as a security for the performance of any covenant or agreement, for the payment or transfer of any sum of money, or annuity, or any share in any of the stocks or funds before mentioned, such bond shall be charged with the same duty as if the same had been immediately given for the payment or transfer of such money or annuity or share of the said stocks or funds.

And where in England any bond for the payment or transfer, or for the performance of any covenant for the payment or transfer, of any sum of money, or annuity, or any share in any of the stocks or funds before-mentioned, shall be contained in one and the same deed or writing with any other matter or thing, specifically charged with any duty (except any declaration of trust of the money, annuity, stock, or fund secured) such deed or writing shall be charged with the same duty as such bond and other matter or thing would have been charged with if contained in separate deeds.

But where in England a bond for the performance of covenants or agreements (other than for the payment or transfer of any sum or annuity or any share in any of the said stocks or funds) shall be contained in the same deed or writing with any other matter or thing, the same shall not be charged separately, but the whole shall be considered as one deed and be charged accordingly under its proper denomination.

205

<br>

### --- Page 0229 ---

APPENDIX.

EXEMPTIONS FROM THE PRECEDING AND ALL OTHER Stamp Duties.

Bonps of the Royal Exchange and London Assurance Corporations exempted from the stamp duty by the act of the sixth year of the reign of King George the First, (a) under which they were incorporated.

Bonps and other securities exempted from stamp duty by the act of the twenty-sixth year of his present majesty’s reign, (b), or any other act now in force for the encouragement of the British fisheries.

Bonps exempted from stamp duty by the act of the twenty-eighth year of his present majesty’s reign, (c), or any other act now in force, relating to the exportation of wool or any manufacture thereof, or fuller’s earth, fulling clay, or tobacco-pipe clay, or by the act of the twenty-ninth year of his present majesty’s reign, (d), or any other act now in force, relating to the exportation of tobacco from his majesty’s warehouses.

Coast Bonps, or bonds relating to the carrying of goods or merchandize coastwise, whether the same shall be given pursuant to the act of the twenty-third of his present majesty’s reign, (e), or any other act now in force for the relief of the coast trade of Great Britain, or pursuant to the directions of any proclamation or orders in council by his majesty’s heirs or successors.

Bonps and other securities, exempted from stamp duty by the act of the thirty-third year of his majesty’s reign, (f), or any other act now in force for the encouragement of friendly societies. (g)

(a) 6 Geo. 1, c. 18, s. 8. (d) 29 Geo. 3, c. 68, s. 41.
(b) 26 Geo. 3, c. 81, s. 31. (e) 32 Geo. 3, c. 50, s. 9, 11.
(c) 12 Geo. 3, c. 38, s. 57. (f) 33 Geo. 3, c. 54, s. 8.
(g) The 9 Geo. 4, c. 92, s. 7, which has repealed the other statutes relating to savings banks, exempts bonds given to trustees or managers of savings banks from stamp duty.

### --- Page 0230 ---

APPENDIX.

Bonps given by cardmakers for securing the stamp duty on playing cards.
Bonps given by the proprietors, printers, or publishers of newspapers for securing the payment of the duties upon advertisements therein contained.
Bonps given by stationers and others, who sell stamped paper for the printing of newspapers, for the due performance of the matters required of them by the act passed in the thirty-eighth year of his present majesty’s reign, (a) for regulating the printing and publication of newspapers.
Bonps given by collectors of assessed taxes and their sureties, for the due payment of monies collected by them or otherwise relating to their offices.
Administration and Confirmation Bonps, given by the widow, child, father, mother, brother; or sister of any common seaman, marine, or soldier, who shall be slain or die in the service of his majesty, his heirs or successors.
Administration Bonp in England, given by any person, where the estate to be administered shall not exceed twenty pounds in value.
Confirmation Bonp in Scotland, where the whole personal estate of the deceased shall not exceed twenty pounds in value.
